(١) قوله : إذ لا مناسبة بين الأرض والعمل الخ وقانون الفقهاء في معرفة التجانس والتناسب أنّ ما صدر فعله من القوى الحيوانية فهو جنس واحد، كالعامل والثور، وما صدر من غيرها فهو جنس آخر كالبذر والأرض . قاله جلبي [٢]قوله : ويجبر من أبى الخ هذا شروع لبيان صفة عقد المزارعة، تكون لازماً أو غيره، وهو إما لازم في حال دون حال، أما بعد إلقاء البذر في الأرض فإنّه لازم من الجانبين، ليس لأحدهما فسخه إلا بعذر. وأما قبله فلازم من جهة من ليس البذر منه ، وغير لازم من جهة من هو منه ، فإن امتنع صاحب البذر لم تجبر عليه؛ لأنه لا يمكنه المضي في العقد إلا بضرر يلزمه، وهو استهلاك البذر في الحال، ولم يدر أنه خارج أم لا. فصار كمن استأجر رجلاً لعدم داره فندم قبله فله أن يرضى به ؛ لأنه امتناع عن إتلاف ملكه، وإن امتنع غيره أجبر الحاكم على العمل ؛ لأنه لا يلحقه بالوفاء بالعقد ضرر سوى ما التزمه بالعقد، وهو إقامة العمل وهو قادر عليها، كما التزمه. فإن قيل : أليس الحال في جانب صاحب البذر كذلك. (۱) في «ذخيرة العقبى»(ص٥٦٦).
Book of sharecropping: its definition and conditions. Splitting the grain and straw (is invalid if it is) for other than the owner of the seed, or splitting the straw and grain for one of them. If one stipulates splitting grain and straw for the owner of the seed, or does not mention straw, it is valid. Likewise if the land and seed are for Zayd and the oxen and work for another, or the land or work for him, and the remainder for another. And it is void if the land and oxen are for Zayd, or the seed and oxen for him and the other two for another, or the seed for him and the remainder for another. Splitting grain and straw for other than the owner of the seed is against the requirement of the contract, or splitting straw and grain for one of them cuts the partnership in what is intended. So if one stipulates splitting grain and straw for the owner of the seed, or does not mention straw, it is valid—because in the first, the condition is required by the contract, for it is growth of his property; and in the second, partnership in what is intended is achieved, and then the straw is for the owner of the seed, and according to some, it is shared following the grain. Likewise if the land and seed are for Zayd and the oxen and work for another, or the land or work for him and the remainder for another. And it is void if the land and oxen are for Zayd, or the seed and oxen for him and the other two for another, or the seed for him and the remainder for another. Know that by rational division it has seven aspects: because either one from one party and three from the other, and this has four aspects: either the land, or work, or seed, or oxen from one, and the rest from the other. The first two are permissible, the third is not due to possibility of riba, and the fourth is not mentioned in al-Hidayah, and it is also not permissible because it is hiring oxen for unknown wages. [1] His saying: 'according to some it is shared'—those some are the scholars of Balkh. It is shared according to them, considering custom where the contracting parties did not specify, and because it follows the grain, and the follower stands by the condition of the original. (1) Raising 'al-tibn' as a conjunction to 'tansif', and likewise his saying: 'and the grain for one of them.' See: Radd al-Muhtar (5:176). (2) They are the scholars of Balkh, and what Sadr al-Shari'ah said is the apparent narration. See: al-Shurunbulaliyah (2:326), and Radd al-Muhtar (5:176). And in al-Durr al-Mukhtar (5:176): 'In Sharh al-Wahbaniyah from al-Qunyah: The sharecropper on a quarter does not deserve anything of the straw, and on a third deserves half.' And if it is valid, the produce is according to the condition, and the worker gets nothing if nothing comes out, and whoever refuses to proceed is compelled except the owner of the seed. And whenever it is void, the produce goes to the owner of the seed, and the other gets the hire of the like of his land or his work, and he is not given more than what was stipulated. And either two from one party and two from the other, and this has three aspects: either land with seed, or with oxen, or with work from one, and the other two from the other. The first is permissible, not the others, for there is no correspondence between land and work, nor between land and oxen. And from Abu Yusuf, may Allah have mercy on him, is the permissibility of this. (And if it is valid, the produce is according to the condition, and the worker gets nothing if nothing comes out, and whoever refuses to proceed is compelled except the owner of the seed) because proceeding involves harm, namely the destruction of seeds. And whenever it is void, the produce goes to the owner of the seed, and the other gets the hire of the like of his land or his work, and he is not given more than what was stipulated. According to Muhammad, he gets whatever it amounts to. [2] (1) His saying: 'for there is no correspondence between land and work...' and the rule of jurists in knowing homogeneity and suitability is that what emanates from animal forces is one type, like the worker and the ox, and what emanates from other than them is another type, like seed and land. Said by Jalabi. [2] His saying: 'and whoever refuses...' This is a beginning to explain the character of the sharecropping contract, whether it is binding or not. It is binding in one state and not in another. As for after sowing the seed in the land, it is binding on both sides, neither may rescind it except for an excuse. As for before that, it is binding on the one who does not own the seed, and not binding on the one who owns it. If the owner of the seed refuses, he is not compelled, because he cannot proceed with the contract without harm to him, namely the immediate consumption of the seed, not knowing whether produce will come or not. So it is like one who hires a man to repair his house and then regrets before that: he may back out because it is refusal to destroy his property. If another refuses, the judge compels him to work, because fulfilling the contract does not cause him harm beyond what he committed to, namely performing the work, and he is capable of it, as he committed. If it is said: isn't the situation regarding the seed owner likewise? (1) From 'Dhakirat al-Uqba' (p. 566).
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition on the contract of sharecropping (المزارعة), defining it and outlining the conditions for its validity and the consequences of void contracts. The author begins by stating that it is invalid to stipulate that the grain and straw (the entire produce) be split for someone other than the owner of the seed (رب البذر), because this contradicts the inherent nature (مقتضى العقد) of a sharecropping contract where the seed belongs to one party. Similarly, it is invalid to split the straw and the grain wholly for one party, as that eliminates the partnership that is fundamental to the contract. However, if one stipulates splitting both grain and straw for the seed owner, it is valid because the condition aligns with the contract's purpose — the produce is the growth of his property. If no stipulation is made regarding straw, the contract is also valid, and the straw belongs to the seed owner by default, though some Hanafi scholars (the Mashayikh of Balkh) consider it shared in proportion to the grain, based on custom. The passage then examines various input combinations (land, seed, oxen, work) for validity. For example, if Zayd provides land and seed while another provides oxen and labor, the contract is valid (جائز). However, if Zayd provides land and oxen (or seed and oxen) and another provides the rest, it is void (باطل) because it resembles rental of oxen for an unknown wage or is subject to riba. A rational division into seven possible combinations is presented, with the rule that only combinations where the seed owner contributes to the growth (land and seed together) are valid. The second part of the passage addresses the rulings after the contract: if valid, the produce is divided as stipulated; if nothing is produced, the worker gets nothing. Any party who refuses to proceed after sowing (except the seed owner) may be compelled by the judge to fulfill the contract. The seed owner is exempt because proceeding would require consuming his seed without guarantee of return. If the contract is void (فسد), the produce belongs to the seed owner, and the other party receives the fair market hire (أجر المثل) for his contributed inputs (land or labor), but not exceeding the amount stipulated in the void contract. Regarding the binding nature of the contract, it becomes binding on both sides only after the seed is sown; before that, it is binding only on the party who does not own the seed. This commentary closely follows the text of Al-Hidayah and other Hanafi sources, clarifying the nuances of sharecropping law.

Key Terms
مزارعة (muzāra‘ah): A contract of sharecropping where one party provides land and seed, and the other provides work (and sometimes oxen), and the produce is shared according to agreement.
رب البذر (rabb al-badhr): The owner of the seed; the party whose property (seed) is used for cultivation.
تبن (tibn): Straw, the byproduct of the grain harvest.
مقتضى العقد (muqtaḍā al-‘aqd): The inherent requirement or logical implication of the contract type; conditions must align with it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following combinations makes a sharecropping contract valid according to the relied-upon Hanafi position?
A) Land from Zayd, seed from Amr, oxen and work from Bakr.
B) Land and seed from Zayd, oxen and work from Amr.
C) Land and oxen from Zayd, seed and work from Amr.
D) Seed and oxen from Zayd, land and work from Amr.

Key Takeaway
The validity of a sharecropping contract hinges on the seed owner also contributing land or work, ensuring that the produce is the growth of his property.

Rulings on Valid and Void Sharecropping: Compensation and Bindingness
Rulings on produce distribution in valid and void contracts, compensation, and binding nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the consequences of a valid vs. void sharecropping contract, including compensation for the worker and the binding nature of the contract before and after sowing.

Core Idea
In a valid contract, produce is shared as agreed; in a void contract, the seed owner takes all produce and compensates the other party at fair market value (not exceeding the stipulated share).

Once the input combinations are settled, the next question is what happens after cultivation. If the contract is valid and yield is nil, the worker gets nothing. Parties may be compelled to perform, but the seed owner can withdraw before sowing due to potential loss. If the contract is void, the produce goes to the seed owner, and the other party receives the hire of the like of his contribution, but not more than the agreed share according to Abu Hanifa, while Muhammad gives the stipulated amount. This lesson also examines the binding nature: after sowing the seed, the contract becomes binding on both sides.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rulings for a valid sharecropping contract
Learn that in a valid contract, the produce is distributed as stipulated. If nothing grows, the worker gets no wage. The contract is enforceable: a refusing party (except the seed owner) can be compelled to proceed.
2
Analyze the compensation rules for a void contract
Understand that in a void contract, the produce belongs to the seed owner. The other party receives the hire of the like (ajr al-mithl) for his land or labor, but not exceeding the stipulated amount according to the Hanafi position; Muhammad disagrees and gives the full stipulated amount.
3
Examine the binding nature of the contract
Before sowing, the contract is binding only on the non-seed owner. The seed owner may withdraw because forcing him would risk his seed. After sowing, the contract becomes binding on both parties, needing an excuse to rescind.
4
Apply the second set of input combinations (two from each)
The text lists three possibilities: land+seed vs oxen+work (valid), land+oxen vs seed+work (invalid per majority, valid per Abu Yusuf), and land+work vs seed+oxen (invalid). Understand the rule of correspondence: land and seed are of one type, work and oxen of another.

Book of sharecropping: its definition and conditions. Splitting the grain and straw (is invalid if it is) for other than the owner of the seed, or splitting the straw and grain for one of them. Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition on the contract of sharecropping (المزارعة), defining it and outlining the conditions for its validity and the consequences of void contracts. The author begins by stating that it is invalid to stipulate that the grain and straw (the entire produce) be split for someone other than the owner of the seed (رب البذر), because this contradicts the inherent nature (مقتضى العقد) of a sharecropping contract where the seed belongs to one party. Similarly, it is invalid to split the straw and the grain wholly for one party, as that eliminates the partnership that is fundamental to the contract. However, if one stipulates splitting both grain and straw for the seed owner, it is valid because the condition aligns with the contract's purpose — the produce is the growth of his property. If no stipulation is made regarding straw, the contract is also valid, and the straw belongs to the seed owner by default, though some Hanafi scholars (the Mashayikh of Balkh) consider it shared in proportion to the grain, based on custom. The passage then examines various input combinations (land, seed, oxen, work) for validity. For example, if Zayd provides land and seed while another provides oxen and labor, the contract is valid (جائز). However, if Zayd provides land and oxen (or seed and oxen) and another provides the rest, it is void (باطل) because it resembles rental of oxen for an unknown wage or is subject to riba. A rational division into seven possible combinations is presented, with the rule that only combinations where the seed owner contributes to the growth (land and seed together) are valid. The second part of the passage addresses the rulings after the contract: if valid, the produce is divided as stipulated; if nothing is produced, the worker gets nothing. Any party who refuses to proceed after sowing (except the seed owner) may be compelled by the judge to fulfill the contract. The seed owner is exempt because proceeding would require consuming his seed without guarantee of return. If the contract is void (فسد), the produce belongs to the seed owner, and the other party receives the fair market hire (أجر المثل) for his contributed inputs (land or labor), but not exceeding the amount stipulated in the void contract. Regarding the binding nature of the contract, it becomes binding on both sides only after the seed is sown; before that, it is binding only on the party who does not own the seed. This commentary closely follows the text of Al-Hidayah and other Hanafi sources, clarifying the nuances of sharecropping law.

Key Terms
أجر المثل (ajr al-mithl): The fair market wage or hire for a similar service or use of property.
لزوم العقد (luzūm al-‘aqd): The binding nature of a contract; after sowing, the sharecropping contract becomes binding (lazim) on both sides.
فساد (fasād): Voidness (fasid) in a contract – it is defective but may produce some legal effects (e.g., compensation).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a void sharecropping contract, according to the relied-upon Hanafi position, the non-seed owner's compensation is:
A) The full amount stipulated, regardless of market rate.
B) The hire of the like (ajr al-mithl) of his contribution, but not more than the stipulated amount.
C) Nothing, because the contract is void.
D) The entire produce minus the seed owner's share.

Key Takeaway
Even in a void sharecropping contract, the non-seed owner receives fair compensation, ensuring that no party is unjustly enriched.

Rulings on Muzara'ah: Validity, Binding Force, and Invalidity Consequences
Validity, binding force, and dissolution of muzara'ah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for a valid muzara'ah contract, identify when it becomes binding, and apply the consequences of invalidity or dissolution under Hanafi fiqh.

Core Idea
After sowing, muzara'ah is binding on both; seed owner cannot revoke without harm.

The passage from the sharh of al-Hidayah explains the sharecropping contract (muzara'ah) in detail. It covers what makes the contract valid or invalid, when each party can withdraw, and how compensation is calculated if the contract fails. Understanding these rules is essential for anyone involved in agricultural partnerships.

Guided Path
1
Identify the valid combinations
The text lists three ways to split contributions (land, seeds, work, oxen). Only the first combination (land+seeds from one party, work+oxen from the other) is valid. Identify why the other two are invalid.
The invalid combos lack suitability (munasaba) between the components.
2
Determine binding force before and after sowing
Before sowing, the seed owner can withdraw; after sowing, both are bound. Understand the rationale: the seed owner would suffer immediate loss if forced to proceed.
The worker is forced to proceed even before sowing.
3
Analyze invalidity consequences
If the contract is invalid (fasid), produce goes to the seed owner, and the other gets ajr mithl. Note the disagreement between Abu Hanifa and Muhammad on whether the wage is capped at the stipulated amount.
Ajr mithl is market rate, but Abu Hanifa caps it at the agreed amount.
4
Examine dissolution scenarios
Death of a party or a debt requiring sale of the land dissolves the contract before crop growth. After crop growth, the land cannot be sold because of the sharecropper's right.
If term expires before harvest, the worker pays rent for his share until harvest.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حسن الدراية لأواخر شرح الوقاية
وإذا صحت، فالخارج على الشرط، ولا شيء للعامل إن لم يخرج، ويجبر من أبى عن المضي إلا رب البذر. ومتى فسدت فالخارج لرب البذر، وللآخر أجر مثل أرضه، أو عمله، ولا يزاد على ما شرط.
وإما أن يكون اثنان من أحدهما، واثنان من الآخر، وهو على ثلاثة أوجه: إما أن يكون الأرض مع البذر، أو مع البقر، أو مع العمل من أحدهما، والباقيان من الآخر. والأول جائز دون الآخرين، إذ لا مناسبة بين الأرض والعمل، وكذا بين الأرض والبقر. وعن أبي يوسف [؟] جواز هذا.
وإذا صحت، فالخارج على الشرط، ولا شيء للعامل إن لم يخرج، ويجبر من أبى عن المضي إلا رب البذر؛ لأن المضي عليه لا يخلو عن ضرر، وهو إهلاك البذور.
ومتى فسدت فالخارج لرب البذر، وللآخر أجر مثل أرضه، أو عمله، ولا يزاد على ما شرط. وعند محمد له بالغا ما بلغ.
قوله: إذ لا مناسبة بين الأرض والعمل...الخ؛ وقانون الفقهاء في معرفة التجانس والتناسب أن ما صدر فعله من القوى الحيوانية فهو جنس واحد، كالعامل والثور، وما صدر من غيرها فهو جنس آخر كالبذر والأرض. قاله جلبي.
قوله: ويجبر من أبى...الخ؛ هذا شروع لبيان صفة عقد المزارعة، تكون لازماً أو غيره، وهو إما لازم في حال دون حال. أما بعد إلقاء البذر في الأرض فإنه لازم من الجانبين، ليس لأحدهما فسخه إلا بعذر. وأما قبله فلازم من جهة من ليس البذر منه، وغير لازم من جهة من هو منه. فإن امتنع صاحب البذر لم تجبر عليه؛ لأنه لا يمكنه المضي في العقد إلا بضرر يلزمه، وهو استهلاك البذر في الحال، ولم يدر أنه خارج أم لا. فصار كمن استأجر رجلاً لعدم داره فندم قبله فله أن يرضى به؛ لأنه امتناع عن إتلاف ملكه. وإن امتنع غيره أجبر الحاكم على العمل؛ لأنه لا يلحقه بالوفاء بالعقد ضرر سوى ما التزمه بالعقد، وهو إقامة العمل وهو قادر عليها، كما التزمه. فإن قيل: أليس الحال في جانب صاحب البذر كذلك.
ولو أبى رب البذر والأرض وقد كرب العامل، فلا شيء له حكماً، ويسترضى ديانة.
وتبطل بموت أحدهما، وتفسخ بدين محوج إلى بيعها. هذا قبل أن ينبت الزرع، لكن يجب ديانة أن يسترضي إذا عمل العامل. أما إذا نبت الزرع ولم يستحصد لا يباع الأرض لتعلق حق المزارع.
فإن مضت المدة ولم يدرك الزرع فعلى العامل أجر مثل نصيبه من الأرض حتى يدرك: أي أجر مثل ما فيه نصيبه، ونفقة الزرع عليهما بالحصص. قلنا: نعم؛ إلا أن في إلزام موجب العقد إياه ضرر فيما يتناوله العقد؛ لأن البذر ليس بمعقود عليه، بخلاف العمل. هذا زبدة ما في «العناية» و«الكفاية».
قوله: وقد كرب؛ أي والحال أنه أي العامل قد قلب الأرض للزرع، كما فهم من «الصحاح».
قوله: وتفسخ بدين محوج إلى بيعها...الخ؛ قال في «الهداية»: فإذا فسخت المزارعة بدين قادح لحق صاحب الأرض فاحتاج إلى بيعها فباع جاز، كما في الإجارة، وليس للعامل أن يطالبه بما كرب الأرض وحفر الأنهار بشيء؛ لأن المنافع إنما تتقوم بالعقد، وهو إنما قوم بالخارج، فإذا انعدم الخارج لم يجب شيء، ولو ثبت الزرع ولم يستحصد.
Husn al-Dirayah li-Awakhir Sharh al-Wiqayah. If it is valid, then the produce is according to the condition, and the worker gets nothing if nothing comes out, and the one who refuses to proceed is forced except the owner of seeds. And if it becomes invalid, the produce belongs to the owner of seeds, and the other gets the equivalent rent of his land or his work, and it is not increased beyond what was stipulated. And either it is that two are from one party and two from the other, and it is of three ways: either the land is with the seeds, or with the oxen, or with the work from one party, and the remaining two from the other. The first is permissible, unlike the other two, because there is no suitability between land and work, and similarly between land and oxen. And from Abu Yusuf [?] its permissibility. And if it is valid, the produce is according to the condition, and nothing for the worker if nothing comes out, and the one who refuses to proceed is forced except the owner of seeds, because proceeding for him does not lack harm, which is the destruction of seeds. And if it becomes invalid, the produce is for the owner of seeds, and the other gets the equivalent rent of his land or his work, and it is not increased beyond what was stipulated. And according to Muhammad, he gets it up to whatever amount. He said: Since there is no suitability between land and work... etc. And the rule of jurists in knowing homogeneity and proportionality is that what is produced by animal powers is one genus, like the worker and the ox, and what is produced by others is another genus, like seeds and land. Jallabi said it. He said: And the one who refuses is forced... etc. This is a commencement to explain the nature of the muzara'ah contract, whether it is binding or not, and it is binding in some circumstances and not in others. After the seeds have been sown in the land, it is binding on both sides; neither of them may rescind it except with an excuse. But before that, it is binding on the one who does not provide seeds, and not binding on the one who does. If the owner of seeds refuses, he is not forced, because he cannot proceed with the contract except with harm to himself, which is the wastage of seeds immediately, and he does not know whether produce will come out or not. So it is like someone who hired a man to demolish his house and then changed his mind before that; he has a right to back out, because it is a refusal to destroy his property. But if someone else refuses, the judge forces him to work, because fulfillment of the contract causes him no harm beyond what he undertook by the contract, which is performing the work—he is able to do it, as he undertook. If it is said: Is not the case on the side of the owner of seeds likewise? And if the owner of seeds and land refuses after the worker has tilled, then he has nothing legally, and he is to be pleased religiously. And the contract is invalidated by the death of one of them, and is dissolved by a debt necessitating its sale. This is before the crop grows, but it is required religiously to please if the worker has worked. However, if the crop has grown but not yet ripened, the land is not sold because of the right of the sharecropper. If the term expires and the crop has not ripened, then the worker owes the equivalent rent of his share of the land until it ripens—that is, the equivalent rent of what his share is in—and the expenses of the crop are on both in proportion. We said: Yes, except that obliging him (the worker) with the contract's requirement causes harm in what the contract covers, because the seeds are not part of the contract, unlike work. This is the summary of what is in al-Inayah and al-Kifayah. He said: And he has tilled; meaning that the worker has turned over the soil for cultivation, as understood from al-Sihah. He said: And it is dissolved by a debt necessitating its sale... etc. He said in al-Hidayah: If the muzara'ah is dissolved due to a detrimental debt that afflicts the landlord so he needs to sell it and sells, it is permitted, as in leasing. And the worker may not demand anything from him for tilling the land and digging the canals, because benefits are only valued by the contract, and it was valued by the produce; so if the produce is not obtained, nothing is due. And if the crop is established but not ripened.
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage from the sharh of al-Hidayah discusses the rulings on muzara'ah (sharecropping) regarding its validity, binding force, and consequences of invalidity or dissolution. The text is a commentary by Husn al-Dirayah on the matn of al-Wiqayah. It begins by stating that in a valid muzara'ah, the produce is distributed as agreed, and the worker gets nothing if no produce results. The owner of seeds is not forced to proceed if he refuses, because forcing him would cause loss of his seeds. If the contract is invalid (fasid), the produce goes to the owner of seeds, and the other party receives the equivalent wage (ajr mithl) for his land or work, not exceeding the stipulated amount. Muhammad holds that the worker gets the full equivalent wage regardless. The text then discusses the types of muzara'ah: where both land and seeds come from one party, or land with oxen, or work from one party and the rest from the other. The first is permissible because land and work are not comparable (one is passive, the other active). Abu Yusuf permits the other two combinations. The commentary then explains the binding nature of the contract: after the seeds are sown, it is binding (lazim) on both parties; before sowing, it is binding only on the one who does not own the seeds (typically the worker), because the seed owner may suffer loss of his seeds. If the seed owner refuses after tilling, the worker has no legal claim but should be compensated religiously. The contract is invalidated by the death of either party and dissolved by a debt that requires selling the land, but only before the crop grows. Once the crop grows, the land cannot be sold due to the sharecropper's right. If the term expires before the crop ripens, the worker must pay rent for his share of the land until harvest, and expenses are shared proportionally. The commentary concludes by citing al-Hidayah that if the contract dissolves due to debt, the worker cannot claim compensation for tilling if the crop fails, because benefits were valued only against the produce.

Key Terms
مزارعة (muzara'ah): A contract of sharecropping where one party provides land and seeds, the other provides work and oxen (or variations), and the produce is shared as agreed.
بذر (badhr): The seeds provided by the seed owner (rabb al-badhr) that are sown in the land.
أجر مثل (ajr mithl): The equivalent market value wage or rent for the land or work, given to the party who contributed when the contract is invalid.
رب البذر (rabb al-badhr): The owner of the seeds in the muzara'ah contract.
العامل (al-'amil): The worker or cultivator in the muzara'ah contract, who typically provides work and sometimes oxen.
كرب (karaba): To till or turn over the soil in preparation for cultivation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a valid muzara'ah contract, if the owner of seeds refuses to proceed after the seeds have been sown, what is the ruling according to the Hanafi school?
A) He is forced by the judge to proceed.
B) He is not forced because of the risk of losing his seeds.
C) The worker must compensate him if he backs out.
D) The contract is automatically dissolved.

Key Takeaway
In muzara'ah, the moment of sowing transforms the contract from revocable to binding, reflecting the Hanafi concern for preserving parties' rights and preventing waste of resources.

Custom's Power: Validating Conditions in Muzara'ah
Dispute on condition of carriage to home based on custom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the condition of carrying the crop to the landowner's home in a muzara'ah contract is invalid by default but valid when supported by custom (ta'amul), and identify which Hanafi imam's view is preferred for fatwa.

Core Idea
A condition benefiting only one party invalidates a contract unless local custom transforms it into an accepted practice.

In Hanafi fiqh, muzara'ah (sharecropping) is a contract validated by custom. However, additional conditions can disrupt its validity. This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute: the default ruling holds that stipulating the worker must carry the crop to the landowner's home invalidates the contract. Yet a transmission from Abu Yusuf, preferred by later scholars like al-Sarakhsi, validates such a condition when local custom supports it. Understanding this dispute clarifies how custom (ta'amul) can override default rulings in Hanafi muamalat.

Guided Path
1
Identify the default Hanafi position
Read the passage to find the ruling that the muzara'ah becomes invalid due to the condition. Note the rationale: it is not required by the contract and benefits one party, analogous to the condition of carrying and grinding.
This is the position from Zahir al-Riwayah.
2
Identify the alternative position from Abu Yusuf
Locate the transmission from Abu Yusuf that the contract is valid and binding if customary practice (ta'amul) supports the condition. Note the analogy to istisna'.
Abu Yusuf's view is based on custom and istihsan.
3
Understand the role of custom (ta'amul)
Analyze how ta'amul changes the ruling. Custom makes the condition acceptable because people transact based on it, similar to the validation of istisna' by custom.
Custom is a source of law in Hanafi fiqh, especially in muamalat.
4
Recognize the authoritative support
Note that al-Sarakhsi and the scholars of Balkh prefer Abu Yusuf's view. Al-Sarakhsi calls it 'the soundest in our lands' due to prevailing custom.
This establishes the mufta bihi position for regions where custom exists.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
Compare the two rulings: where custom exists, the condition is valid and the contract is sound; where custom does not exist, the condition invalidates the contract. This affects enforceability in different localities.
The dispute has real-world impact on contractual practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعن أبي يوسف له أنه يصح ولزمه للتعامل. قال الإمام السَّرَخْسِي: هذا هو الأصح في ديارنا لوقوع التعامل. فسدت المزارعة لأنه شرط لا يقتضيه العقد وفيه منفعة لأحدها فيفسد العقد كشرط الحمل والطحن على العامل. قاله الجلبي. قوله: وعن أبي يوسف له إنه يصح؛ أي أن المزارعة تصح إذا شرط ذلك على العامل للتعامل اعتباراً بالاستصناع، وهو اختيار مشايخ بلخ، قال الزَّيْلَعِي: وهم يزيدون على هذه الرواية ويقولون: يجوز شرط التبعية والحمل إلى منزله على العامل للتعامل. قال الشارح: قال شمس الأئمة السَّرَخْسِي: وهذا هو الأصح في ديارنا.
And from Abu Yusuf [it is narrated] that it is valid and binding due to customary practice. Imam al-Sarakhsi said: This is the soundest in our lands due to the existence of customary practice. The muzara'ah becomes invalid because it is a condition not required by the contract and contains benefit for one of them, so it invalidates the contract like the condition of carrying and grinding on the worker. Al-Jalabi said: His statement: 'And from Abu Yusuf that it is valid' means that the muzara'ah is valid if that is stipulated on the worker due to customary practice, by analogy with istisna', and this is the choice of the scholars of Balkh. Al-Zayla'i said: They add to this narration and say: It is permissible to stipulate what is subsidiary and the carrying to his house on the worker due to customary practice. The commentator said: Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi said: This is the soundest in our lands.
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning a condition in a muzara'ah (sharecropping) contract. The default ruling is that if the landowner stipulates that the worker must carry the crop to the landowner's home, the contract is invalid because this condition benefits the landowner alone and is not required by the contract. However, a transmission from Abu Yusuf holds that if local custom (ta'amul) supports such a condition, it is valid and binding. Al-Sarakhsi prefers this view as the soundest in his region due to prevailing custom. The scholars of Balkh also adopt this, analogizing to istisna' (manufacturing contract). The passage clarifies that the condition, though normally invalid, becomes valid when custom is established. The mufta bihi (operative position) is the view of Abu Yusuf and Sarakhsi.

Key Terms
التعامل (al-ta'amul): Customary practice or mutual dealings among people that have become prevalent.
المزارعة (al-muzara'ah): A sharecropping contract where one party provides land and the other provides labor, with the crop divided according to agreement.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred view in this passage, what is the basis for validating the condition of carrying the crop to the landowner's house?
A) Explicit text from Quran or Sunnah
B) Consensus of the Companions
C) Customary practice (ta'amul)
D) Necessity (darurah)

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi muamalat, custom (ta'amul) can transform an otherwise invalid condition into a valid one, as seen in the dispute over carriage in muzara'ah; the relied-upon view of Abu Yusuf and al-Sarakhsi adopts this principle.

Work Classification in Muzara'ah by Time Period
Classification of work responsibilities in muzara'ah based on time relative to harvest.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify work responsibilities in a muzara'ah contract into the three time periods (before maturity, after maturity before division, after division) and identify which stipulations invalidate the contract.

Core Idea
Work before crop maturity is on the worker; after maturity before division is shared; after division is individual.

This passage from Al-Hidayah and its commentary establishes a clear classification for who bears the cost and labor of different tasks in a sharecropping (muzara'ah) contract. The classification hinges on two events: when the crop matures (idrak) and when it is divided (qismah). Understanding this distribution is crucial for drafting valid contracts and avoiding impermissible stipulations that go against established custom.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three time periods
Read the passage and note the three key time periods: before maturity (idrak), after maturity but before division (qismah), and after division. These correspond to the categories of work with different responsibility allocations.
The Arabic terms: الإدراك, القسمة.
2
Determine the responsibility for each period
For each time period, extract the ruling: before idrak – work is on the worker alone; after idrak before qismah – work is shared according to shares; after qismah – work is on each party individually. Confirm these from the text.
Examples: السقي والحفظ (before idrak); الحصاد والدياس (after idrak before qismah); الحمل والطحن (after qismah).
3
Identify impermissible stipulations
Find the two specific prohibitions: stipulating harvesting (a post-idrak work) on the worker alone is impermissible by agreement; stipulating it on the landowner alone is impermissible by consensus. Understand why – lack of custom (العرف).
The text uses 'بالاتفاق' and 'بالإجماع' which indicate unanimity.
4
Recognize the analogy to musaqah
Note that the same classification applies to musaqah (sharecropping of trees) by analogy (على قياس هذا). Understand that the principle is broader than just muzara'ah.
The passage says 'والمعاملة: أي المساقاة على قياس هذا'.
5
Summarize the commentary's extra distinction
The commentary emphasizes that only the first category (work before idrak) is considered part of the muzara'ah contract. The other two fall outside, meaning they are not automatically covered by the contract's compensation structure.
This helps clarify that post-idrak work is additional and can be negotiated separately, but any stipulation against custom is invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي «الهداية»: فالحاصل أن ما كان من عمل قبل الإدراك كالسقي والحفظ فهو على العامل، وما كان منه بعد الإدراك قبل القسمة فهو عليهما على الاشتراك على قدر الحصص في ظاهر الرواية، كالحصاد والدياس وأشباههما على ما بيناه، وما كان بعد القسمة فهو عليهما. والمعاملة: أي المساقاة على قياس هذا ما كان قبل إدراك الثمر من السقي والتلقيح والحفظ، فهو على العامل، وما كان بعد الإدراك كالجداد والحفظ فهو عليهما. فالحاصل أن كل عمل قبل الإدراك فهو على العامل، وما بعده فعليهما بالحصص. ولو شرط الجداد على العامل لا يجوز بالاتفاق؛ لأنه لا عرف فيه، وما كان بعد القسمة فهو عليهما؛ لأنه مال مشترك ولا عقد، ولو شرط الحصاد في الزرع على رب الأرض لا يجوز بالإجماع لعدم العرف فيه. [١]قوله: فالحاصل... الخ؛ يعني إن الأعمال ثلاثة: الأول: ما كان قبل الإدراك كالحفظ والسقي وكري الأنهار فهو على العامل. والثاني: ما كان بعد الإدراك قبل القسمة كالحصاد ونحوه فهو عليهما على الاشتراط. والثالث: ما كان بعد القسمة كالحمل إلى البيت والطحن، فهو على كل واحد منهما في نصيبه خاصة. الأول معدود من أعمال المزارعة لا الآخران.
And in al-Hidayah: The summary is that whatever work is before maturity, like watering and guarding, is on the worker. What is after maturity before division is on both jointly according to their shares in the apparent narration, like harvesting, threshing, and similar as we explained. What is after division is on both. And the mu'amalah (i.e., musaqah) is by analogy with this: what is before maturity of the fruit, like watering, pollinating, and guarding, is on the worker; what is after maturity, like picking and guarding, is on both. So the summary is that every work before maturity is on the worker, and what is after it is on both according to shares. And if harvesting is stipulated on the worker, it is not permissible by agreement because there is no custom in it. What is after division is on both because it is joint property and there is no contract. And if harvesting in cultivation is stipulated on the landowner, it is not permissible by consensus due to lack of custom. [1] His statement: The summary... meaning that works are three: first, what is before maturity like guarding, watering, and digging canals, is on the worker. Second, what is after maturity before division like harvesting and similar, is on both by stipulation. Third, what is after division like carrying to the house and grinding, is on each one in his share specifically. The first is counted among the works of muzara'ah, not the other two.
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage from al-Hidayah and its commentary provides a clear classification of work responsibilities in a muzara'ah contract. Three time periods are distinguished: (1) Work before the crop matures (idrak), such as watering, guarding, and canal digging—this falls entirely on the worker (the farmer). (2) Work after maturity but before the crop is divided, such as harvesting and threshing—this is shared between the landowner and worker according to their agreed shares. (3) Work after division, such as carrying the crop home and grinding—each party handles his own share. The passage also notes that stipulating harvesting (a post-maturity task) on the worker alone is impermissible by agreement (of the schools) because there is no custom to support it. Similarly, stipulating harvesting on the landowner alone is impermissible by consensus. The commentary emphasizes that only the pre-maturity work is considered part of the muzara'ah contract; post-maturity and post-division work fall outside the scope of the contract. This classification is based on the apparent narration (zahir al-riwayah) of Imam Muhammad.

Key Terms
الإدراك (al-idrak): The time when the crop reaches maturity and is ready for harvest.
القسمة (al-qismah): The division of the harvested crop between the landowner and worker according to their shares.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling if the landowner stipulates that the worker alone must harvest the crop?
A) Valid, because harvesting is a necessary work
B) Invalid by agreement, because there is no custom for it
C) Valid, by analogy with pre-maturity work
D) Invalid only if the worker objects

Key Takeaway
Work responsibility in muzara'ah is time-dependent: pre-maturity on the worker, post-maturity before division shared, post-division individual; stipulations against custom are invalid.

Classification of Tasks in Muzara'ah
Summarizing the division of labor in sharecropping

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three categories of tasks in a muzara'ah contract and identify who bears the cost for each category according to Hanafi law.

Core Idea
In muzara'ah, tasks before ripening are on the worker; after ripening before division, shared by stipulation; after division, each party for himself.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah clarifies the allocation of tasks in a sharecropping (muzara'ah) contract. The division is based on the timing of tasks relative to crop ripening and division. Understanding this classification is essential for drafting valid contracts and avoiding invalid stipulations that contradict customary practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three temporal boundaries
Look for the key time periods: before ripening, after ripening but before division, and after division.
2
List the tasks for each period
For each period, note the specific tasks mentioned: guarding, watering, digging canals (before ripening); harvesting (after ripening before division); carrying to house, grinding (after division).
Tasks may vary by region; these are examples.
3
State the rule for each
Before ripening: worker's sole responsibility. After ripening before division: shared proportionally as per stipulation. After division: each party bears his own tasks.
The rule before ripening is default; after ripening can be altered by stipulation.
4
Note invalid stipulations
The passage gives examples: stipulating date harvesting (jidad) on the worker is invalid by agreement due to lack of custom; similarly for crop harvesting on the landowner.
Invalidity is based on absence of customary practice (urf).
5
Recognize what counts as muzara'ah work
Only tasks before ripening are considered part of the muzara'ah contract; the other two categories are outside the contract's scope.
This affects whether a condition is considered part of the contract or an additional stipulation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالحاصل أنَّ كُلَّ عمل قبل الإدراك ، فهو على العامل، ومـا بعـده فعليهما بالحصص. ولو شرط الجداد على العامل لا يجوز بالاتفاق ؛ لأنه لا عرف فيه، وما كان بعد القسمة فهو عليهما ؛ لأنه مال مشترك ولا عقد، ولو شرط الحصاد في الزرع على ربّ الأرض لا يجوز بالإجماع لعدم العرف فيه.
[١]قوله : فالحاصل.... الخ ؛ يعني إن الأعمال ثلاثة :
الأول : ما كان قبل الإدراك كالحفظ والسقي وكري الأنهار فهو على العامل.
والثاني : ما كان بعد الإدراك قبل القسمة كالحصاد ونحوه فهو عليهما علـى الاشتراط.
والثالث : ما كان بعد القسمة كالحمل إلى البيت والطحن، فهو على كل واحدٍ منهما في نصيبه خاصة.
الأوّل معدود من أعمال المزارعة لا الآخران، قال الزاهدي : معنى الطيب في قوله : كُلُوا مِمَّا فِي الْأَرْضِ حَلَالًا طَيِّبًا ) : إنّ من زرع الأرض محافظاً علــى الصلاة في مواقيتها بالجماعة فهو طيّب، لكنّه لو أخّر الصلاة عن وقتها بالزراعة لا يكون زرعه طيباً. وكذا لو زرع أو غرس بغير طهارة أو منع الأجرة من الأجير أو أخره بعدما جف عرفه، وكذا إذا أخر أداء الثمن بعد حلول الأجل أو أداه متفرقاً بدون رضاء البائع ، ويستحب أن يقع البذر على الطهارة، ثمّ يصلي ركعتين ثم يقول : أنا عبد ضعيف سلّمت إليك هذا، فسلّمه لي، وبارك لي فيه، ثمّ يصلي على النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم ، فإن الله يحفظ هذا الزرع عن آفاته، ويبارك فيه. قاله الجلبي "
Thus, every task before ripening is the responsibility of the worker, and after it is upon both proportionally. If date harvesting is stipulated on the worker, it is not valid by agreement because there is no customary practice in it. And what is after division is upon both because it is joint property without a contract. If harvesting of crops is stipulated on the landowner, it is not valid by consensus due to lack of customary practice.
[1] His statement: 'Thus... etc.' means that tasks are three: First: what is before ripening, like guarding, watering, and digging canals—it is on the worker. Second: what is after ripening before division, like harvesting and similar—it is on both according to stipulation. Third: what is after division, like carrying to the house and grinding—it is on each one in his own portion. The first is counted among the tasks of muzara'ah, not the other two. Al-Zahidi said: The meaning of 'good' in His statement: 'Eat from the earth lawful and good' is that whoever plants land while maintaining prayer in its times with congregation is good, but if he delays prayer from its time due to planting, his crop is not good. Likewise, if he plants or cultivates without purity, or prevents the wage from the hired worker, or delays it after its custom has dried, and similarly if he delays payment of the price after the term has come or pays it in installments without the seller's consent. It is recommended that the seed fall with purity, then he prays two rak'ahs, then says: 'I am a weak servant, I surrender this to You, so safeguard it for me and bless me in it,' then sends blessings on the Prophet, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, for Allah will protect this crop from its afflictions and bless it. This was said by al-Jalabi.
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the commentary on Al-Wiqayah, explaining the classification of tasks in a muzara'ah (sharecropping) contract. The author divides tasks into three categories: (1) tasks before ripening (e.g., guarding, watering, digging canals) are solely the responsibility of the worker (the one who cultivates); (2) tasks after ripening but before division (e.g., harvesting) are shared between the landowner and the worker based on their stipulation; (3) tasks after division (e.g., carrying home, grinding) are each party's own responsibility for their share. The text emphasizes that stipulating a task contrary to custom is invalid. The latter part quotes al-Zahidi, who explains that the verse 'eat lawful and good' implies not neglecting prayer for farming, and recommends performing the planting with purity, prayer, and supplication for blessing. This is a practical elaboration of the earlier ruling, not a new legal rule.

Key Terms
الإدراك (al-idrak): Ripening of the crop, the point after which the fruit is ready for harvest.
الجداد (al-jidad): The act of harvesting dates or fruits, specifically cutting them from the tree.
الحصاد (al-hisad): Harvesting of field crops like grains, cutting them at maturity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a muzara'ah contract, who is responsible for the cost of digging canals according to the passage?
A) The landowner
B) The worker
C) Both equally
D) It depends on the stipulation

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the division of tasks in muzara'ah is determined by the stage of the crop: before ripening (worker), after ripening (shared), after division (individual).

Definition and General Ruling of Musaqat
Defining musaqat and comparing it to muzara'ah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define musaqat, state the Hanafi fatwa on its validity, and identify its conditions and differences from muzara'ah.

Core Idea
Musaqat is valid by fatwa, akin to muzara'ah, but seed specification is irrelevant.

This lesson introduces the Book of Musaqat with a concise definition and a comparative analysis with muzara'ah. The text presents the Hanafi ruling, the intra-madhhab disagreement between Abu Hanifa and his two companions, and the cross-madhhab view of Imam al-Shafi'i. Understanding this foundation is crucial for grasping the detailed conditions and practical applications that follow.

Guided Path
1
Define musaqat linguistically and legally
The text defines musaqat as handing over trees to a worker for a share of the fruit. Note the key elements: object (trees), action (tending), and consideration (share of fruit).
2
Identify the ruling and intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
The fatwa is on validity (sahih), even though Abu Hanifa considered it batil. The two companions (Sahibayn) held it valid; their view is the mufta bihi.
The dispute is based on qiyas vs. istihsan.
3
List the conditions common to musaqat and muzara'ah
Conditions include eligibility of parties, specification of the worker's share, handing over the trees to the worker, and partnership in the produce. The text notes that seed specification does not apply in musaqat.
4
Contrast with muzara'ah and cross-madhhab dispute
For Hanafis, both contracts are independently valid. For Shafi'is, muzara'ah is only valid within a musaqat contract. Understand the practical consequence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

فقط ، وفي المزارعةِ لا تجوز الشركة في مجرِّدِ الربح، وهو ما زاد على البذر.
Book of Musaqat: It is the handing over of trees to one who tends them for a share of their fruit. And it is like muzara'ah in ruling, difference, and conditions. For the ruling of musaqat is the ruling of muzara'ah in that the fatwa is on its validity, and that it is void according to Abu Hanifa (may Allah be pleased with him) contrary to the two [companions], and that its conditions are like its conditions in every condition that can exist in musaqat, like the eligibility of the contracting parties, clarification of the worker's share, handing over the trees to the worker, and partnership in the produce. As for clarification of the seed and the like, it is not possible in musaqat. According to al-Shafi'i, musaqat is permissible, and muzara'ah is only permissible within musaqat, because the origin is mudarabah, and musaqat is more similar to it because partnership is only in the profit, and in muzara'ah, partnership in the mere profit, i.e., what exceeds the seed, is not permissible.
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage is the opening of the Book of Musaqat, defining the contract and comparing it to muzara'ah. The definition states that musaqat is the act of giving trees to a worker who will tend them in exchange for a share of the fruit. The ruling: The fatwa (operative position) is that musaqat is valid, even though Imam Abu Hanifa considered it void (batil) based on qiyas. His two companions, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, held it valid, and their view is the basis for fatwa. The conditions of musaqat are similar to those of muzara'ah wherever they apply: the parties must be legally capable, the worker's share must be specified, the trees must be handed over, and there must be partnership in the produce. However, conditions like specifying the seed (bathr) are impossible in musaqat since the trees already exist. Imam al-Shafi'i also permits musaqat but restricts independent muzara'ah, allowing it only as part of a musaqat contract. The commentary explains that musaqat is related to muzara'ah and that the Prophet's transaction with the people of Khaybar is evidence for its validity. It also clarifies that partnership in musaqat is only in the profit (the increase), not the principal, and that in muzara'ah, partnership in the mere profit (excluding the seed) is not permitted.

Key Terms
المساقاة (al-musāqāt): A contract where trees are given to a worker to tend in exchange for a share of the fruit.
المزارعة (al-muzāra‘ah): Sharecropping of land where the landowner provides land and the worker cultivates for a share of the crop.
الشَّركة في الخارج (al-shirkah fī al-khārij): Partnership in the produce (the actual fruit or crop).
الربح (al-ribḥ): Profit, i.e., the increase over the principal (in musaqat, the fruit; in muzara'ah, the crop after deducting seed).
البذر (al-badhr): Seed; in muzara'ah, the seed is often provided by one party, and its specification is a condition for validity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi fatwa, how is musaqat classified?
A) Batil (void) according to all Hanafi imams
B) Valid (sahih) according to the fatwa, though Abu Hanifa considered it batil
C) Valid only if the duration is specified
D) Makruh (disliked) but valid

Key Takeaway
Musaqat is a valid contract by Hanafi fatwa, akin to muzara'ah but without the seed condition, and the mufta bihi follows the two companions over Abu Hanifa.

Duration in Musaqat and the Four Differences with Muzara'ah
Explaining the exception of duration in musaqat via istihsan

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on specifying duration in musaqat and list the four differences between musaqat and muzara'ah.

Core Idea
In musaqat, duration need not be specified because fruit ripening has a known time, by istihsan overriding qiyas.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya explains a key exception in the contract of musaqat (tree-tending): unlike muzara'ah, the duration can be left unspecified. The Hanafi school relies on istihsan (juristic preference) because the ripening time of fruit is naturally known, making specification unnecessary. The text also contrasts musaqat with muzara'ah in four specific rulings, highlighting the distinct legal treatment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling on duration
Read the opening lines: the duration in musaqat is valid without being mentioned. Note that this is an exception to the general qiyas that would require it.
2
Understand the proof: istihsan over qiyas
The justification is 'istihsan' because the ripening of fruit has a known and consistent time (idrak al-thamar). Qiyas would analogize musaqat to a lease (ijarah), requiring a specified term, but istihsan prevails due to custom.
Compare with muzara'ah where duration must be specified because crop times vary.
3
List the four differences with muzara'ah
Al-Zayla'i enumerates four points: (1) compulsion to continue, (2) treatment after term ends, (3) compensation if trees are reclaimed, and (4) the duration rule itself. Memorize these distinctions.
The fourth difference is the subject of the passage: duration is not required in musaqat but is required in muzara'ah.
4
Apply the ruling to a scenario
Example: A palm grove owner and a worker agree on musaqat (worker tends trees for a share of fruit) without stating an end date. The contract is valid; it ends when fruit is harvested. The worker cannot be forced to continue indefinitely, but if he refuses, he may be compelled because no harm.
This contrasts with muzara'ah where the seed-owner cannot be compelled.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إلا المدة ; فإنها تصح بلا ذكرها
حسن الدراية لأواخر شرح الوقاية
فقط ، وفي المزارعةِ لا تجوز الشركة في مجرِّدِ الربح، وهو ما زاد على البذر. (إلا) المدة ; فإنها تصح بلا ذكرها , استحساناً , فإنَّ لإدراكِ الثَّمر وقتا معلوماً شرط الشركة في الربح دون البذر بأن شرط رفعه من رأس الخارج يفسد، فجعلنا المعاملة أصلاً، وجوزنا المزارعة تبعاً لها : كالشرب في بيع الأرض، والمنقول في وقف العقار. كذا في «الهداية»(۱).
١ ] قوله : استحساناً في «الهداية»(۳) : وفي الاستحسان : إذا لم يبين المدة يجوز ويقع على أول ثمر يخرجُ ; لأنَّ الثمر لإدراكها وقت معين معلوم، وقلما يتفاوت ويدخل فيها ما هو المتيقن ، وإدراك البذر في أصول الرطبة في هذا بمنزلة إدراك الثمار ؛ لان له نهاية معلومة، فلا يشترط بيانُ المدة، وشرط المدّة قياس فيها ؛ لأنها إجارة معنى

(r)d
«الجلالية»، قال الزَّيْلَعِي (۳) : هي كالمزارعة في جميع ما ذكرنا إلا في أربعة أشياء : أحدها : إذا امتنع أحدهما يجبر عليه ; لأنه لا ضرر عليه في المضي، بخلاف المزارعة حيث لا يجبر صاحب البذر إذا امتنع. والثاني : إذ انقضت المدة يترك بلا أجر، ويعمل بلا أجر، وفي المزارعة بأجر على

Except the duration: it is valid without mentioning it, by istihsan, because fruit has a known time for ripening. ... [context from text]
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage deals with the exception of duration (al-mudda) in musaqat. Unlike muzara'ah, where the duration must be specified, in musaqat the contract is valid even if the duration is not mentioned. This is by istihsan (juristic preference) because the ripening of fruit has a known and predictable time, unlike crops which can be planted in different seasons. The analogy (qiyas) would require specification, but istihsan prevails due to prevalence of such transactions. The text then lists four differences between musaqat and muzara'ah as noted by al-Zayla'i: (1) If one party refuses, he is compelled to continue in musaqat because there is no harm, whereas in muzara'ah the seed-owner is not compelled. (2) After the term ends, the worker in musaqat continues without rent (i.e., he works for free until fruit is harvested), while in muzara'ah rent is due after the term. (3) If the trees are reclaimed (due to a rightful claim), the worker receives the equivalent wage (ujr al-mithl) in musaqat, whereas in muzara'ah the worker receives the value of the crop. (4) The duration itself is the fourth difference, as mentioned in the book.

Key Terms
المدة (al-muddah): The duration or term of the contract; in musaqat, it need not be specified.
الاستحسان (al-istiḥsān): Juristic preference that overrides qiyas due to practical necessity or custom.
القياس (al-qiyās): Analogical reasoning; in this case, qiyas would require specifying duration because the contract is similar to a lease (ijarah).
إدراك الثمر (idrāk al-thamar): The ripening of fruit; considered a known time for contract termination.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the duration not required to be specified in musaqat according to the Hanafi school?
A) Because qiyas requires it to be unspecified
B) Because the ripening of fruit has a known time, and istihsan overrides qiyas
C) Because the contract is a gift, not a lease
D) Because both parties always agree on the duration orally

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, musaqat is valid without a stated duration because fruit ripening provides a certain end, overriding the qiyas that would analogize it to a lease.

The Duration in Musaqat: Istihsan Overrides Qiyas
Explaining the ruling on duration in musaqat and its conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the duration requirement in musaqat, explain the istihsan basis, and identify the conditions that invalidate the contract.

Core Idea
Musaqat is valid without a specified duration because fruit ripening is known by custom.

In Hanafi fiqh, musaqat (sharecropping of trees) is a flexible contract that adapts to agricultural realities. Unlike ijarah, it does not require a fixed term. This lesson unpacks the ruling from al-Hidayah, showing how istihsan permits this flexibility and what conditions still govern the contract.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Ruling
Recognize that the passage addresses the duration (muddah) in musaqat. The ruling: the contract is valid (sahih) without specifying the duration.
2
Understand the Qiyas Objection
Some scholars analogize musaqat to ijarah, which requires a fixed term. This is the qiyas argument that the text refutes.
Qiyas is the strict analogy.
3
Apply Istihsan
The text invokes istihsan because fruit ripening has a known time by custom. This makes the duration effectively known, bypassing the need for explicit mention.
Custom (urf) is given legal weight.
4
Note Conditions for Invalidity
If a duration is mentioned that is too short for the fruit to ripen, the contract is invalid. If no fruit appears in the natural season, the contract is void.
The contract falls on the first fruit, not beyond.

وتقع على أول ثمر يخرج، وإدراكُ بِذرِ الرّطبة كإدراك النمر، وذكر مدةٍ لا يخرج به النمر فيها يفسدها ، ومدة قد يبلغ فيها وقد لا
(وتقع على أول ثمر ) يخرج ، وإدراكُ بِذرِ الرطبة كإدراك النمر)، الرطبة بالفارسية : سيست تر، فإنَّه إذا دَفَعَ الرَّطبة مساقاة لا يشترط بيان المدة فيمتد إلى إدراك بذر الرطبة ؛ فإنَّه كإدراكِ الثَّمرِ في الشجر.
أقول : الغالب أنَّ البذرَ فيها غير مقصود ، بل يُحْصَدُ في كلِّ سنة ست مرات أو أكثر، فإن أريد البذرُ يحصد مرّة، ويترك في المرة الثانية إلى أن يُدرك البذر ففيما لا يؤخذ البذر ينبغي أن يقع على السنة الأولى : أي على السنة التي تنتهي الرطبة
فيها بعد العقد.
(وذكر مدة لا يخرج به الشِّمرُ فيها يفسدها، ومدة قد يبلغ فيها وقد لا (۱) قوله : وتقع على أوّل ثمر ...الخ ؛ أي في أوّل السنة ؛ لأنَّ لإدراك الثمر وقتاً معلوماً عادة، والثابت عادة كالثابت شرعاً ، فصارت المدة معلومة، وإن تقدّم أو تأخر، يسير لا يقع بسببه منازعة عادة.

Except the duration; for it is valid without mentioning it, by istihsan, because the ripening of the fruit has a known time. The condition of partnership in profit without the seed, such that the condition of removing it from the capital invalidates, so we make the transaction original and permit muzara'ah as subsidiary to it: like the right of watering in the sale of land, and moveable property in waqf of real estate. Thus in al-Hidayah. 1. His saying: 'by istihsan' in al-Hidayah: In istihsan: if the duration is not specified, it is valid and falls on the first fruit that emerges, because the fruit has a known, fixed time for ripening, and it rarely varies, and what is certain is included. The ripening of the seed in the roots of ratbah is like the ripening of fruits in this regard, because it has a known end, so specifying the duration is not required. Stipulating the duration is analogically required, because it is a lease in meaning, as in muzara'ah. 2. His saying: 'because the ripening of the fruit has a time...etc.'; unlike crops, because among people there are those who plant in autumn, those who plant in spring, and those who plant in summer. When the start of work can be early or late, the completion is also similar, so the duration becomes unknown and it is not permissible. Thus in al-Jalaliyah. Al-Zayla'i said: It (musaqat) is like muzara'ah in all that we have mentioned except in four things: first, if one of them refuses, he is compelled, because there is no harm in proceeding, unlike muzara'ah where the owner of the seed is not compelled if he refuses. Second, when the period ends, he leaves without wage and works without wage, but in muzara'ah it is with wage as we explained. Third, if the date palms are claimed, the worker gets the equivalent wage, and in muzara'ah he gets the value of the crop. Fourth, the duration, and it is mentioned in the book, said al-Jalabi. And it falls on the first fruit that comes out, and the ripening of the seed of ratbah is like the ripening of dates. Mentioning a duration in which the fruit does not come out invalidates it, and a duration in which it may or may not be attained. (And it falls on the first fruit that comes out, and the ripening of the seed of ratbah is like the ripening of dates). Ratbah in Persian is 'sistar'. For if he gives the ratbah as musaqat, specifying the duration is not required; it extends to the ripening of the seed of ratbah, for it is like the ripening of fruit on trees. I say: Mostly the seed in it is not intended; rather it is harvested six times or more per year. If the seed is intended, it is harvested once and left the second time until the seed ripens. So for that which does not have the seed taken, it should fall on the first year: i.e., the year in which the ratbah ends after the contract. (And mentioning a duration in which the fruit does not come out invalidates it, and a duration in which it may or may not be attained). His saying: 'and it falls on the first fruit...etc.'; i.e., in the first year, because the ripening of the fruit has a known time by custom, and what is established by custom is like what is established by law, so the duration becomes known, even if it is slightly early or late, no dispute usually arises because of it. And we are certain that the contract applies to the first fruit that comes out, and beyond that there is doubt, so only what is certain is established, until they said: Musaqat is invalid if the fruit does not come out in that year, because the contract does not apply to other than that year, as if they contracted for that. Said al-Jalabi. 2. His saying: 'ratbah' - with fatha: isfist. In Kitab al-'Ushr: vegetables other than rattab; vegetables like leeks and the like, and rattab is cucumber, watermelon, eggplant and the like. The first is what is mentioned in my lexicons. Thus in al-Mughrib. 3. His saying: 'the fruit does not come out in it...etc.'; as if the contract between them is to plant trees that do not produce fruit within that duration, like vines in one year, for example. Said Jalabi. (1) In Dhakirat al-'Uqba (p. 568). (2) Al-Mughrib (p. 190). (3) In Dhakirat al-'Uqba (p. 568).
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the ruling regarding the duration in musaqat (sharecropping of trees). The author begins by stating the key exception: unlike other conditions in musaqat, the duration needs not be specified for the contract to be valid, by way of istihsan (juristic preference). The reasoning is that the ripening of fruit has a known time, so the duration is effectively known even if not explicitly stated. This is contrasted with muzara'ah (sharecropping of crops), where the duration must be specified because planting times vary. The passage then explains four differences between musaqat and muzara'ah, as noted by al-Zayla'i: (1) In musaqat, if one party refuses, he can be compelled to proceed, unlike muzara'ah where the seed owner is not compelled. (2) When the duration ends, the worker in musaqat leaves without wage and works without wage (i.e., no further compensation), but in muzara'ah the worker receives a wage for extra work. (3) If the trees are claimed by a third party, the worker gets the equivalent wage, whereas in muzara'ah he gets the value of the crop. (4) The duration itself is considered as part of the contract but is not specified. The passage then discusses the application of musaqat to ratbah (green plants). When ratbah is given in musaqat, the duration is not required because the ripening of its seed is analogous to fruit. However, if a duration is mentioned that is too short for the fruit to mature, the contract is invalid. If the duration is such that the fruit may or may not mature within it, the contract is valid and falls on the first fruit. The commentary also notes that custom establishes the time of ripening, and that the contract only covers the first fruit—if no fruit comes out in that year, the contract is void.

Key Terms
المساقاة (al-musaqat): A contract of sharecropping in which the owner gives trees or vines to a worker to care for in exchange for a share of the fruit.
المدة (al-muddah): The period of time for which the musaqat contract runs. The text rules that it does not need to be explicitly stated.
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): Juristic preference that allows departing from a strict analogy (qiyas) for a more equitable ruling. Here it permits not specifying duration.
الرطبة (al-ratbah): A type of green plant or grass, also referred to as 'sfisf' (alfalfa). It is harvested multiple times a year and its seed ripening is analogous to fruit.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what happens if a musaqat contract is made without specifying a duration?
A) The contract is invalid because the term is unknown.
B) The contract is valid and falls on the first fruit that ripens.
C) The contract is valid indefinitely until terminated.
D) The contract is valid only if the fruit ripens within one year.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, musaqat is valid without a specified duration because custom makes the ripening time known, and istihsan permits the contract to fall on the first fruit.

The Scope of Musaqah: Valid Trees and Plants
Explains conditions for valid musaqah and trees it applies to

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on the types of trees and plants valid for musaqah, contrast it with the Shafi'i position, and identify the condition regarding fruit ripeness.

Core Idea
Musaqah is valid in all trees and plants by istihsan, not only in palms and vines.

This passage from a Hanafi commentary on Al-Wiqayah addresses a key point of disagreement between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools: which plants can be the subject of a musaqah contract? While the Shafi'i school restricts it to palm trees and grapevines based on the Khaybar hadith, the Hanafi school extends it to all trees, vegetables, and roots by istihsan (juristic preference) due to common need. Understanding this dispute reveals how the Hanafi school prioritizes public welfare (hajat al-nas) in contractual matters.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify what the Hanafi school says about the types of plants valid for musaqah: all trees, vines, vegetables, roots of eggplant, and palms.
The text lists 'karm' (vine), 'shajar' (trees), 'rattab' (vegetables), 'usul al-badhinjan' (roots of eggplant), and 'nakhl' (palm).
2
Identify the Shafi'i opposition
Note that Imam al-Shafi'i restricts musaqah to palm trees and grapevines only, relying on the hadith of Khaybar.
The hadith only mentions palms and vines, so for Shafi'is, other plants remain under general qiyas which does not permit them.
3
Explain the Hanafi response using istihsan
Understand that the Hanafi school uses istihsan (juristic preference) to extend the ruling beyond the explicit text due to widespread need (hajat al-nas).
Istihsan overrides qiyas in this case because the need for musaqah in all trees is pressing.
4
Note the condition on fruit ripeness
Identify that musaqah is invalid if the fruit is already fully ripe (mudrik) at the time of contract, because work is only needed before ripening.
This is similar to muzara'ah: it is valid for greens but not for harvested crops.
5
Consider the incomplete final ruling
The passage begins to discuss what happens if one party dies or the term expires while the fruit is unripe (ni), but it is cut off. The implication is that the worker continues, as the contract does not terminate automatically.
The missing part likely states that the worker completes the work or gets a share estimate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يصح فلو خرج في وقتِ سُمِّي فعلى الشرط ، وإلا فللعامل أجر المثل، وتصح في الكرم والشجر، والرّطاب وأصول الباذنجان، والنخل وإن كان فيه ثمر وإلا مدركاً كالمزارعة هذا عندنا، وعند الشَّافِعِي لا تصح إلا في الكرم والنخل، وإنما تصح فيهما بحديث خيبر، وفي غيرهما بقي على القياس، وعندنا تصح في جميع ما ذكر لحاجةِ النَّاس، ثُمَّ إذا صحت تصح وإن كان الثمر على الشَّجر إلا أن يكونَ الثَّمر مدركاً؛ لأنه يحتاج إلى العمـل قـبل الإدراك لا بعده كالمزارعة تصح إذا كان بقلا، ولا تصح إذا استحصد، لكن إجارة الأرض لا تصح إلا وأن تكون خالية عن زرع المالك. فإن مات أحدهما، أو مضت مدتها والثَّمر ني يقوم العامل عليه أو
It is valid. So if (the fruit) comes out in the specified time, then (the contract is) on the condition, otherwise the worker gets the equivalent wage. And it is valid for vines, trees, vegetables, roots of eggplant, and palm trees even if there is fruit on them unless it is ripe, like muzara'ah. This is according to us. And according to al-Shafi'i, it is not valid except for vines and palm trees. It is only valid in these two by the hadith of Khaybar, and in others it remains on analogy. And according to us, it is valid in all that was mentioned due to the need of the people. Then, when it is valid, it is valid even if the fruit is on the trees unless the fruit is ripe, because work is needed before ripening, not after, just as muzara'ah is valid if it is greens, and is not valid if it is harvested. But the leasing of land is not valid unless it is free of the owner's crop. If one of them dies, or its term passes and the fruit is unripe, the worker continues on it or...
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the types of trees in which musaqah (a partnership in trees where one party works and the other provides trees/land) is valid. The Hanafi school holds that musaqah is valid for all types of trees and plants, including vines, fruit trees, vegetables, roots of eggplant, and palm trees. This is based on general need (istihsan). The Shafi'i school restricts it to palm trees and vines only, relying on the specific hadith of Khaybar where the Prophet contracted with the Jews of Khaybar for their palm trees and land. The Hanafi response is that the need of people extends to other trees as well, so the ruling is extended by istihsan. The text also clarifies a condition: the fruit must not be fully ripe (mudrik) at the time of contract, because work is needed before ripening. This is similar to muzara'ah (sharecropping on land) where the crop must not be fully harvested. Finally, the rule for death or expiry of term while fruit is unripe (ni) is mentioned: the worker continues, implying the contract is not terminated.

Key Terms
مساقاة (musaqah): A contract of partnership in trees where one party provides the trees/land and the other provides labor, in exchange for a share of the fruit.
مدرك (mudrik): Fruit that has reached full ripeness and is ready to harvest.
ني (niyy (ni)): Unripe fruit that has not reached its full potential ripeness, e.g., green dates (busr akhdar).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, in which of the following is musaqah valid?
A) Only in palm trees
B) Only in palm and grapevines
C) In all trees and plants
D) Only in trees that produce fruit

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school validates musaqah in all trees and plants by istihsan, as long as the fruit is not fully ripe at the contract's start.

Definition and Validity of Musaqat (Hanafi Position)
Definition and inter-madhhab dispute on Musaqat scope

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the validity of Musaqat contracts and explain the inter-madhhab dispute with the Shafi'i school.

Core Idea
Musaqat is valid on all trees in Hanafi school, based on istihsan for public need, unlike Shafi'i restriction to date palms and grapevines.

This passage defines the contract of Musaqat and immediately presents the divergence between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools. Understanding this dispute is key to grasping the role of istihsan in Hanafi fiqh and the practical flexibility it provides.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contractual definition
Read the first line to understand what Musaqat is: a contract where the owner gives trees to a worker for tending in exchange for a share of fruit.
Note the contrast with Muzara'ah (sharecropping) mentioned.
2
Locate the Shafi'i position and its proof
Find the statement 'عند الشافعي لا تصح إلا في الكرم والنخل' and its evidence: the hadith of Khaybar.
The hadith only mentions date palms and land.
3
Identify the Hanafi response and its basis
The Hanafi school says 'وعندنا تصح في جميع ما ذكر لحاجة الناس' – validity in all trees due to public need, overriding analogy via istihsan.
Istihsan is a key Hanafi source.
4
Note the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
In Hanafi fiqh, a Musaqat is valid on fig, pomegranate, etc.; in Shafi'i, only on date palms and grapevines.
This affects fatwa and practice.
