(۱) السعف : ورق جريد النخل الذي يسوى منه الزبل والمراوح ، وعن الليث : أكثر ما يقال له : السعف إذا يبس، وإذا كانت رطبة فهي الشَّطْبة، وقد يقال للجريد نفسه سعف الواحدة سعفة. ينظر: «المغرب» (ص ٢٢٥ - ٢٢٦).
(۲) خبر المبتدأ الذي هو كون العامل. ينظر : فتح باب العناية» (٢ : ٥٥٥). (۳) أي أرضاً بيضاء غير مغروسة. ينظر : فتح باب العناية»(٥ : ٥٥٥).
(٤) (العناية» (۹) : ٤٨١).
(٥) في «ذخيرة العقبى»(ص٥٦٩).
(٦) «المغرب»(ص۳۳۹).
Book of Musaqat / Its Definition and Rulings. 243. If there is fruit in it, otherwise [the fruit is] ripe, like muzara'ah. If one of them dies, or its duration passes and the fruit is unripe, the worker continues on it. This is according to us, but according to al-Shafi'i, it is not valid except in grapevines and date palms; it is only valid in these two due to the hadith of Khaybar, and in other than them it remains on analogy. According to us, it is valid in all that was mentioned due to the need of people. Then, if it is valid, it is valid even if the fruit is on the tree, unless the fruit is ripe; because it needs work before ripeness, not after, like muzara'ah: it is valid if it is [still] greens, and not valid if it is fully ripe. But leasing land is not valid unless it is empty of the owner's crop. If one of them dies, or its duration passes and the fruit is unripe, the worker continues on it. [Footnote: His saying: 'niy' - with a kasra on the noon and tashdid on the ya - that which has not reached its perfection in anything, like uncooked meat. What is meant here is the fruit that is not fully mature, like green dates. Thus says al-Jalabi. His saying: 'the worker continues' - as it was. If the worker commits to take his share, his heirs may choose between dividing the produce according to the condition, giving him the value of his share, or spending on it until it matures and then recouping from the worker's share. If both die, the choice goes to the worker's heirs, as we explained. If they refuse, then the landowner's heirs have three options as described. He did not comment on 'or passes' because the ruling of the expiry of the term when the fruit is unripe is the same as the death of one of them. This is the essence of what is in al-Hidayah and al-Inayah. Said by al-Jalabi.] [Page 244] ... and his heir, even if the one who gave [the land] or his heirs dislike it, and it is not rescinded except due to an excuse. The worker being sick and unable to work, or being a thief who is feared for his [palm] leaves or fruit, is an excuse. And giving empty land for a specified period to plant, with the land and trees between them, is not valid. The fruit and the planting belong to the owner... [Commentary: 'Istihsanan' - The analogy is that the musaqat is terminated between them, and the unripe dates would be divided between the landowner's heirs and the worker halves if they stipulated halves, because the landowner hires the worker for a portion of the produce, and a hire contract is terminated by the death of one of the parties. Thus in al-Inayah. His saying: 'on his sa'f' - with a fatha on the sin and 'ayn and the fa - it is the leaf of the date palm branch, and is sometimes used for the branch itself from which baskets are made. What is meant here is the second meaning. Said by Akhi al-Jalabi. His saying: 'al-ghars...' - with a fatha on the ghayn and sukun on the ra: what is planted. It has also been read with a kasra, from their saying: 'he takes his planting.' Thus in al-Mughrib.]
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition on the contract of Musaqat (irrigation/tending of trees for a share of the fruit). It begins by defining the contract and then immediately contrasts the Hanafi position with that of Imam al-Shafi'i. The Shafi'i school restricts Musaqat to date palms and grapevines, relying on the hadith of Khaybar where the Prophet (peace be upon him) contracted with the Jews of Khaybar for the date palms and land. The Hanafi school, however, extends the validity to all types of fruit trees and even to grapevines and other plants, basing this on the principle of 'need of the people' (hajat al-nas) which is a recognized basis for istihsan (juristic preference). The text then clarifies an essential condition for validity: the fruit must be on the tree but not yet ripe (niy, meaning unripe). This is analogous to the Muzara'ah (sharecropping) contract where the crop must still be green. The reason given is that the work needed is before ripening, not after. If the fruit is already ripe, the contract is not valid because no work remains. The passage then addresses the scenario of the death of one of the parties or the expiry of the contract term while the fruit is still unripe. In such cases, the worker (or his heirs) may continue to tend the fruit until it ripens, even if the owner or his heirs object. This is based on istihsan to prevent harm. The contract cannot be rescinded except for a valid excuse, such as the worker becoming sick and unable to work, or being a thief who might steal the fruit. The final part discusses giving empty land (fada') for a specified period to plant, making the land and trees shared between them—this is not valid as a Musaqat contract because it involves partnership in something that already exists before the contract. Instead, it would be a different type of contract. The commentary (from al-Jalabi and others) explains key terms: 'niy' and 'sa'f', and clarifies the legal reasoning behind the rulings, such as the analogy (qiyas) vs. istihsan in the case of death.

Key Terms
مساقاة (musāqāt): A contract where the owner of trees gives them to a worker to tend and irrigate in exchange for a share of the fruit.
استحسان (istiḥsān): Juristic preference; a legal method that deviates from strict analogy for public benefit or custom.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, on which types of trees is a Musaqat contract valid?
A) Only date palms and grapevines
B) Only fruit trees that require irrigation
C) All types of trees and vines, including pomegranates and figs
D) Only trees that bear fruit twice a year

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school validates Musaqat on all trees based on istihsan and public need, in contrast to the Shafi'i restriction.

Condition for Validity: Fruit Must Be Unripe (Niy)
Condition of unripe fruit (niy) for Musaqat validity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the condition that fruit must be unripe (niy) for a valid Musaqat and explain the analogy to Muzara'ah.

Core Idea
Musaqat is valid only if the fruit is still unripe (niy); if ripe, no work remains and the contract is invalid.

The text now elaborates on a key condition: the fruit must be on the tree but not yet ripe. This ensures that the worker actually has work to do. This condition is parallel to the Muzara'ah contract where the crop must still be green.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition
Read: 'ثُمَّ إذا صحت تصح وإن كان الثمر على الشَّجر إلا أن يكونَ الثَّمر مدركاً' - the contract is valid with fruit on tree unless it is ripe (mudrik).
'Mudrik' means fully ripe.
2
Understand the rationale
The reason given: 'لأنه يحتاج إلى العمل قبل الإدراك لا بعده' - work is needed before ripening, not after.
If the fruit is already ripe, there is nothing left for the worker to do.
3
Compare with Muzara'ah
The text draws an analogy: Muzara'ah is valid with green plants, not when fully grown.
This reinforces the principle that the worker's labor must be meaningful.

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition on the contract of Musaqat (irrigation/tending of trees for a share of the fruit). It begins by defining the contract and then immediately contrasts the Hanafi position with that of Imam al-Shafi'i. The Shafi'i school restricts Musaqat to date palms and grapevines, relying on the hadith of Khaybar where the Prophet (peace be upon him) contracted with the Jews of Khaybar for the date palms and land. The Hanafi school, however, extends the validity to all types of fruit trees and even to grapevines and other plants, basing this on the principle of 'need of the people' (hajat al-nas) which is a recognized basis for istihsan (juristic preference). The text then clarifies an essential condition for validity: the fruit must be on the tree but not yet ripe (niy, meaning unripe). This is analogous to the Muzara'ah (sharecropping) contract where the crop must still be green. The reason given is that the work needed is before ripening, not after. If the fruit is already ripe, the contract is not valid because no work remains. The passage then addresses the scenario of the death of one of the parties or the expiry of the contract term while the fruit is still unripe. In such cases, the worker (or his heirs) may continue to tend the fruit until it ripens, even if the owner or his heirs object. This is based on istihsan to prevent harm. The contract cannot be rescinded except for a valid excuse, such as the worker becoming sick and unable to work, or being a thief who might steal the fruit. The final part discusses giving empty land (fada') for a specified period to plant, making the land and trees shared between them—this is not valid as a Musaqat contract because it involves partnership in something that already exists before the contract. Instead, it would be a different type of contract. The commentary (from al-Jalabi and others) explains key terms: 'niy' and 'sa'f', and clarifies the legal reasoning behind the rulings, such as the analogy (qiyas) vs. istihsan in the case of death.

Key Terms
ني (niy): Unripe fruit that has not reached its full maturity, like green dates.
مدرك (mudrik): Fully ripe fruit that does not need further work.

What is the condition regarding the fruit for a valid Musaqat contract?
A) The fruit must be ripe

Key Takeaway

Effect of Death or Expiry of Term during Unripe Fruit
Ruling on death/expiry with unripe fruit

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the ruling when one party dies or the contract term expires while the fruit is still unripe, and distinguish the Hanafi istihsan from qiyas.

Core Idea
If the fruit is unripe when term ends or a party dies, the worker or his heirs continue until ripeness, based on istihsan to prevent harm.

This passage addresses a practical problem: what happens if the worker dies or the contract term expires while the fruit is still unripe? The Hanafi school, by istihsan, rules that the worker (or heirs) may continue to tend the fruit until it ripens, overriding the general analogy that the contract would terminate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Read: 'فإن مات أحدهما ، أو مضت مدتها والثمر نى يقوم العامل عليه' – if one dies or term expires while fruit is unripe, the worker continues.
This is the basic ruling.
2
Understand the istihsan basis
The commentary contrasts qiyas (analogy) which would terminate the contract, but istihsan keeps it going to prevent harm to the worker.
Har is loss of his labor if contract is voided.
3
Apply to both death and term expiry
The text says the ruling is the same for both: the worker (or his heirs) must be allowed to finish.
Also if both die, the worker's heirs have the option.

Book of Musaqat / Its Definition and Rulings. 243. If there is fruit in it, otherwise [the fruit is] ripe, like muzara'ah. If one of them dies, or its duration passes and the fruit is unripe, the worker continues on it. This is according to us, but according to al-Shafi'i, it is not valid except in grapevines and date palms; it is only valid in these two due to the hadith of Khaybar, and in other than them it remains on analogy. According to us, it is valid in all that was mentioned due to the need of people. Then, if it is valid, it is valid even if the fruit is on the tree, unless the fruit is ripe; because it needs work before ripeness, not after, like muzara'ah: it is valid if it is [still] greens, and not valid if it is fully ripe. But leasing land is not valid unless it is empty of the owner's crop. If one of them dies, or its duration passes and the fruit is unripe, the worker continues on it. [Footnote: His saying: 'niy' - with a kasra on the noon and tashdid on the ya - that which has not reached its perfection in anything, like uncooked meat. What is meant here is the fruit that is not fully mature, like green dates. Thus says al-Jalabi. His saying: 'the worker continues' - as it was. If the worker commits to take his share, his heirs may choose between dividing the produce according to the condition, giving him the value of his share, or spending on it until it matures and then recouping from the worker's share. If both die, the choice goes to the worker's heirs, as we explained. If they refuse, then the landowner's heirs have three options as described. He did not comment on 'or passes' because the ruling of the expiry of the term when the fruit is unripe is the same as the death of one of them. This is the essence of what is in al-Hidayah and al-Inayah. Said by al-Jalabi.] [Page 244] ... and his heir, even if the one who gave [the land] or his heirs dislike it, and it is not rescinded except due to an excuse. The worker being sick and unable to work, or being a thief who is feared for his [palm] leaves or fruit, is an excuse. And giving empty land for a specified period to plant, with the land and trees between them, is not valid. The fruit and the planting belong to the owner... [Commentary: 'Istihsanan' - The analogy is that the musaqat is terminated between them, and the unripe dates would be divided between the landowner's heirs and the worker halves if they stipulated halves, because the landowner hires the worker for a portion of the produce, and a hire contract is terminated by the death of one of the parties. Thus in al-Inayah. His saying: 'on his sa'f' - with a fatha on the sin and 'ayn and the fa - it is the leaf of the date palm branch, and is sometimes used for the branch itself from which baskets are made. What is meant here is the second meaning. Said by Akhi al-Jalabi. His saying: 'al-ghars...' - with a fatha on the ghayn and sukun on the ra: what is planted. It has also been read with a kasra, from their saying: 'he takes his planting.' Thus in al-Mughrib.]
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition on the contract of Musaqat (irrigation/tending of trees for a share of the fruit). It begins by defining the contract and then immediately contrasts the Hanafi position with that of Imam al-Shafi'i. The Shafi'i school restricts Musaqat to date palms and grapevines, relying on the hadith of Khaybar where the Prophet (peace be upon him) contracted with the Jews of Khaybar for the date palms and land. The Hanafi school, however, extends the validity to all types of fruit trees and even to grapevines and other plants, basing this on the principle of 'need of the people' (hajat al-nas) which is a recognized basis for istihsan (juristic preference). The text then clarifies an essential condition for validity: the fruit must be on the tree but not yet ripe (niy, meaning unripe). This is analogous to the Muzara'ah (sharecropping) contract where the crop must still be green. The reason given is that the work needed is before ripening, not after. If the fruit is already ripe, the contract is not valid because no work remains. The passage then addresses the scenario of the death of one of the parties or the expiry of the contract term while the fruit is still unripe. In such cases, the worker (or his heirs) may continue to tend the fruit until it ripens, even if the owner or his heirs object. This is based on istihsan to prevent harm. The contract cannot be rescinded except for a valid excuse, such as the worker becoming sick and unable to work, or being a thief who might steal the fruit. The final part discusses giving empty land (fada') for a specified period to plant, making the land and trees shared between them—this is not valid as a Musaqat contract because it involves partnership in something that already exists before the contract. Instead, it would be a different type of contract. The commentary (from al-Jalabi and others) explains key terms: 'niy' and 'sa'f', and clarifies the legal reasoning behind the rulings, such as the analogy (qiyas) vs. istihsan in the case of death.

Key Terms
استحسان (istiḥsān): Juristic preference; deviation from analogy for necessity or benefit.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a Musaqat contract expires while the fruit is unripe, what happens?
A) The contract is void and the owner gets all fruit
B) The worker continues until the fruit ripens
C) The fruit is divided immediately
D) A new contract must be made

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, if the term ends or a party dies while fruit is unripe, the worker or his heirs complete the work by istihsan.

Excuses for Rescission and Invalid Forms of Musaqat
Excuses and invalid form of Musaqat

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify valid excuses for rescinding a Musaqat contract and explain why giving empty land to plant is not a valid Musaqat.

Core Idea
Musaqat cannot be rescinded except for serious excuses like worker sickness or theft; giving empty land for planting is a different contract, not Musaqat.

The final part of the passage discusses when a Musaqat can be rescinded and what constitutes an invalid form. The contract is binding unless there is a valid excuse, and the scenario of giving empty land for planting with shared ownership is not considered a Musaqat.

Guided Path
1
Identify valid excuses
Read: 'ولا تفسخ إلا بعذر' – not rescinded except for excuse. Examples: worker sick unable to work, or thief who may steal leaves/fruit.
These excuses make continuation harmful or impossible.
2
Understand the invalid form
Read: 'ودفع فضاء مدة معلومة ليغرس، ويكون الأرض والشجر بينهما لا يصح' – giving empty land for a fixed period to plant, with land and trees shared, is not valid.
Reason: The partnership in existing land/trees would contradict the nature of Musaqat, which is based on the owner's existing trees.
3
Learn the consequence
If such an invalid form is used, it is not a Musaqat; it might be a different contract (e.g., partnership or lease).
The fruit and plants go to the owner according to the text.

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition on the contract of Musaqat (irrigation/tending of trees for a share of the fruit). It begins by defining the contract and then immediately contrasts the Hanafi position with that of Imam al-Shafi'i. The Shafi'i school restricts Musaqat to date palms and grapevines, relying on the hadith of Khaybar where the Prophet (peace be upon him) contracted with the Jews of Khaybar for the date palms and land. The Hanafi school, however, extends the validity to all types of fruit trees and even to grapevines and other plants, basing this on the principle of 'need of the people' (hajat al-nas) which is a recognized basis for istihsan (juristic preference). The text then clarifies an essential condition for validity: the fruit must be on the tree but not yet ripe (niy, meaning unripe). This is analogous to the Muzara'ah (sharecropping) contract where the crop must still be green. The reason given is that the work needed is before ripening, not after. If the fruit is already ripe, the contract is not valid because no work remains. The passage then addresses the scenario of the death of one of the parties or the expiry of the contract term while the fruit is still unripe. In such cases, the worker (or his heirs) may continue to tend the fruit until it ripens, even if the owner or his heirs object. This is based on istihsan to prevent harm. The contract cannot be rescinded except for a valid excuse, such as the worker becoming sick and unable to work, or being a thief who might steal the fruit. The final part discusses giving empty land (fada') for a specified period to plant, making the land and trees shared between them—this is not valid as a Musaqat contract because it involves partnership in something that already exists before the contract. Instead, it would be a different type of contract. The commentary (from al-Jalabi and others) explains key terms: 'niy' and 'sa'f', and clarifies the legal reasoning behind the rulings, such as the analogy (qiyas) vs. istihsan in the case of death.

Key Terms
سَعَف (saʿf): Leaves or branches of date palm; used in the context of theft.
فَضَاء (fadāʾ): Empty land; uncultivated land.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT a valid excuse for rescinding a Musaqat contract?
A) The worker becomes sick and cannot work
B) The worker is a thief and may steal fruit
C) The owner decides to sell the land
D) The worker is unable to work due to illness

Key Takeaway
A Musaqat is binding and can only be rescinded for serious excuses; giving empty land to plant is a separate invalid form.

Continuity of Musaqah Upon Death and Valid Excuses
Ruling with proof on death and excuses in musaqah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the continuity of a musaqah contract upon the death of either party and identify the valid excuses that permit rescission.

Core Idea
In Hanafi fiqh, a musaqah contract survives the death of either party by istihsan, unless a valid excuse like illness or theft exists.

Musaqah is a contract to tend date palms or vines for a share of the produce. A key question is what happens when one party dies. In this passage from the commentary on al-Wiqayah, the author explains that by istihsan, the contract continues with the heirs to prevent hardship, but a valid excuse may allow rescission. Let's explore the ruling and its proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule stated in the matn
Read the main text: the contract continues with the worker's heirs after his death, and with the worker after the owner's death, even if the other party's heirs object.
The rule is an exception to the normal qiyas.
2
Distinguish the two scenarios
If the worker dies, his heirs stand in his place and continue the work. If the owner dies, the worker continues as before. In both cases, the contract is not rescinded unless there is an excuse.
3
Understand the basis: istihsan vs qiyas
The commentator explains that by strict qiyas (analogy to ijarah), death would annul the contract because ijarah is voided by death. However, istihsan (juristic preference) is applied to prevent harm and preserve the contract.
Istihsan overrides qiyas in this case.
4
List the valid excuses for rescission
Two excuses are mentioned: (1) the worker is sick and unable to work, (2) the worker is a thief and there is fear for the leaves or fruit. These allow the contract to be rescinded.
Only these excuses permit rescission; no other reasons are accepted.
5
Apply the ruling to a hypothetical scenario
Consider a case: a worker dies, and the owner's heirs want to cancel. According to the rule, they cannot cancel unless the worker's heir is sick or a thief. The contract continues by istihsan.
Practice applying the rule to ensure understanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the commentary on al-Wiqayah regarding the continuity of the musaqah (irrigation) contract upon the death of either party. The main text states that if the worker dies, the contract continues with his heirs, and if the landowner dies, the worker continues, even if the heirs of the deceased dislike this. This is based on istihsan (juristic preference) to avoid hardship. However, if a valid excuse exists—such as the worker being chronically ill or the worker being a thief who may steal the fruit—the contract may be rescinded. The commentator then explains that by qiyas (analogy), the contract should be annulled at death because it is akin to a hire (ijarah) contract, which is voided by death. But istihsan prevails to protect the interests of both parties. The ruling is from the Zahir al-Riwayah and is the relied-upon position.

Key Terms
مساقاة (musāqāh): A contract to care for date palms or vines in return for a share of the produce.
استحسان (istiḥsān): Juristic preference that sets aside a strict qiyas in favor of a more equitable ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Only if the landowner's heirs dislike it.
B) Only if the worker is sick and unable to work, or is a thief feared for the fruit.
C) Only if the landowner dies.
D) The contract can never be rescinded.

Key Takeaway
A musaqah contract survives the death of a party by istihsan, and rescission is only permitted for valid excuses like illness or theft.

Invalidity of Fixed-Term Planting Contract and the Hila
Ruling on invalidity and the hila

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a fixed-term planting contract with shared produce ownership, explain its invalidity via analogy to the miller's qafiz, and describe the legal stratagem (hila) that renders a similar arrangement valid.

Core Idea
A contract to share ownership of land and trees after planting is invalid; but a sale of half the trees for half the land plus a hire contract avoids the problem.

This passage addresses a common transaction: a landowner gives fallow land for a fixed period for another to plant trees, with the understanding that both land and trees will be shared equally afterwards. The Hanafi school rules this contract invalid because it creates partnership in property that already exists (the land) before the partnership. The text explains the reasoning via analogy and then provides a legal stratagem (hila) to achieve the same economic outcome validly. Understanding this ruling is important for structuring agricultural partnerships and avoiding invalid contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contract and its invalidity
The contract is: A gives fallow land to B for a fixed period so B can plant trees, with the condition that after planting, the land and trees are owned equally by A and B. The ruling: this contract is not valid (la yasih).
The invalidity is not absolute invalidity (batil) but rather fasid (defective), as the text later assigns rights to both parties.
2
Understand the reason for invalidity
The reason is 'stipulating partnership in what exists before partnership' — i.e., the land is already owned solely by A, so sharing ownership of the land after planting is an invalid condition in a contract that should be about labor or future produce.
The text uses the phrase 'li-ishtirat al-sharika fima huwa hasil qabl al-sharika'.
3
Recognize the analogy to the miller's qafiz
The contract is compared to the miller's qafiz: a miller hires someone to grind wheat and pays with part of the flour before it is produced. That is invalid because it is hiring with uncertain future payment. Similarly, here the worker is hired for his labor and paid with part of the orchard (future produce and land share), which is uncertain and involves partnership in pre-existing property.
This analogy (qiyas) is explicit in the text: 'li-annahu fi ma'na qafiz al-tahan'.
4
Learn the legal stratagem (hila)
To make a similar arrangement valid: The landowner sells half the trees (which the worker will plant) for half the land, making the worker co-owner of the land. Then the landowner hires the worker for a small wage to work on his own (the landowner's) half for a fixed period. This transforms the single invalid contract into two valid contracts: a sale and a hire.
The hila is described in the text: 'Hilat al-jawaz an yabi'a nisf al-aghras bi-nisf al-ard, wa yasta'jir...'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ودفع فضاء مدة معلومة؛ ليغرس، ويكون الأرض والشجر بينهما لا يصح؛ لاشتراط الشركة فيما هو حاصل قبل الشركة. والنمر والغرس لرب الأرض، وللآخر قيمة غرسه وأجر عمله. (والنمر والغرس لرب الأرض، وللآخر قيمة غرسه وأجر عمله)؛ لأنه في معنى قفيز الطحان؛ لأنه استئجار ببعض ما يخرج من عمله، وهو نصف البستان، وإنما لا يكون الغرس لصاحبه؛ لأنه غرس برضاه ورضى صاحب الأرض، فصار تبعاً للأرض. وحيلة الجواز أن يبيع نصف الأغراس بنصف الأرض، ويستأجر صاحب الأرض العامل ثلاث سنين مثلاً بشيء قليل؛ ليعمل في نصيبه. [٣] قوله: والغرس... الخ؛ - بفتح الغين المعجمة، وسكون الراء المهملة. المغروس، وقد جاء فيه الكسر، ومنه قولهم: يأخذ غرسه. كذا في «المغرب». [١] قوله: لأنه غرس برضاه... الخ؛ ولأنه تعذر، والغرس لاتصالهما بالأرض، فإنه لو قلع الغرس وسلّمها لم يكن تسليماً للشجرة، بل يكون تسليماً خشبة، ولم يكن مشروطاً، بل المشروط تسليم الشجر بقوله: على أن يكون الأرض والشجر بين رب الأرض والغارس نصفين. وفي فتاوى قاضي خان: رجل دفع إلى رجل أرضاً مدة معلومة على أن يغرس المدفوع إليه فيها أغراساً على أن ما يحصل من الأغراس أو الثمار يكون بينهما جاز، فيوفق بينه وبين قول المصنف.
And giving fallow land for a fixed period to plant, with the land and trees being between them, is not valid, because it stipulates partnership in what exists before partnership. And the fruit [or produce] and the trees belong to the landowner, and the other [party] gets the value of his trees and his labor wage. (And the fruit [or produce] and the trees belong to the landowner, and the other gets the value of his trees and his labor wage); because it is like the case of the miller’s kafiz (measure); because it is hiring for part of what comes out of his work, which is half the orchard. And the trees do not belong to the planter because he planted with his consent and the landowner’s consent, so they become subservient to the land. The legal stratagem to make it permissible is that he sells half the trees for half the land, and the landowner hires the worker for three years, for example, for a small amount, so that he works in his portion. [3] He said: 'and the trees... etc.' - with fatha on the ghayn and sukun on the ra, meaning planted things. [1] He said: 'because he planted with his consent... etc.'; and because it is impossible, and the trees are attached to the land; if he uproots them and hands them over, he would not be handing over a tree but wood, and this was not stipulated. What was stipulated is handing over the tree by saying: 'on condition that the land and trees are between the landowner and the planter in halves.' And in Fatawa Qadikhan: if a man gives land for a known period for another to plant trees on condition that what comes of the trees or fruit is between them, it is permissible, so it is reconciled with the author’s statement.
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling on an invalid contract structure and provides a legal stratagem (hila) to achieve a similar result validly. The main text states that if someone gives fallow land for a fixed period for the recipient to plant trees, with the understanding that both land and trees will be shared between them after planting, the contract is invalid. The reason is that it stipulates partnership in something that already exists before the partnership (i.e., the land is owned by the giver, and the new trees are created by the worker, but the contract tries to share ownership improperly). In such a case, the fruit (or produce) and the trees belong entirely to the landowner, and the worker only receives the value of his trees and his wages for labor. The commentator explains that this is analogous to the 'qafiz of the miller'—an invalid transaction where payment is made with part of the produce before it is actually produced. The trees become fixtures on the land due to both parties' consent, so they belong to the landowner. To avoid this invalidity, a hila is offered: the landowner sells half the trees for half the land, effectively making the worker a co-owner of the land, and then the landowner hires the worker to work on his (the landowner's) share for a small wage over a fixed period. This turns the transaction into separate valid contracts of sale and hire. The commentator also cites a fatwa from Qadikhan that reconciles another permissible scenario with the author's ruling.

Key Terms
حيلة (ḥīlah): A legal stratagem to achieve a permissible result while avoiding an invalid transaction.
فضاء (fiḍāʾ): Fallow or empty land without trees or crops.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the legal stratagem to make valid a contract where a landowner gives land for a fixed period to plant trees with shared ownership?
A) The landowner sells half the trees for half the land and hires the worker for his share.
B) The landowner gives the land as a gift.
C) The landowner and worker form a partnership.
D) The worker pays rent for the land.

Key Takeaway
A fixed-term planting contract with future shared ownership of land and trees is fasid in Hanafi law; the resulting trees belong to the landowner, but the worker can recover costs and wages, and the parties can use a sale-and-hire hila to achieve a valid arrangement.

Legal Stratagem for Validating Musaqah with Shared Trees
Ruling with qiyas analogy and hila solution

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi legal stratagem (ḥīlah) to convert a fasid musaqah contract into a valid one and justify it using the qiyas of the miller's qafiz.

Core Idea
A musaqah with shared ownership of trees becomes a sale and hire to avoid gharar.

In this passage, we examine a common problem in musaqah: when a worker plants trees on the owner's land and they agree to share both land and trees, the contract is fasid because it involves hiring for an uncertain share of produce. The Hanafi school offers a legal stratagem (ḥīlah) to restructure the transaction into a valid sale and lease. Understanding this stratagem is crucial for practitioners who need to structure agricultural partnerships in accordance with Shariah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Original Arrangement's Problem
The agreement is that the worker plants trees and both share the land and trees. The Hanafi ruling is that this is hiring for a part of the produce, which is uncertain (gharar) and thus fasid. The planter only gets the value of his work and trees, not ownership.
The analogy is with the miller's qafiz—hiring a miller with a portion of the flour is valid only under certain conditions.
2
Understand Why Trees Become Subordinate to Land
Since the planter planted with consent, the trees become tābiʿ (subordinate) to the land. If the contract is invalid, he cannot demand the trees as trees; he only gets their value as wood and his labor wages.
This prevents the planter from claiming ownership of the trees separately.
3
Recognize the Legal Stratagem (Ḥīlah)
The stratagem: the planter sells half the trees to the landowner in exchange for half the land. Then the landowner hires the planter for a nominal wage to tend the entire garden for a fixed period (e.g., three years).
This converts the single invalid contract into two valid ones: a sale of trees for land, and a separate hire contract.
4
Apply the Qiyas to Justify the Stratagem
The original problem is analogous to hiring with uncertain payment (like the miller's qafiz). The stratagem removes the uncertainty by fixing the wage and separating the ownership transfer.
The Hanafi school accepts this hila because it achieves a legitimate end without violating the letter of the law.
5
Note the Authority Supporting the Ḥīlah
The text cites Fatawa Qadi Khan, which permits a similar arrangement where the worker plants trees on the owner's land and shares the produce. This shows that later Hanafi jurists upheld such stratagems.
Though not from Zahir al-Riwayah, it is considered reliable (fatawa mutakhkhirin).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

الأرض، وللآخر قيمة غرسه وأجر عمله

Commentary
This passage from the book of Musaqah discusses a specific case: one person provides land, another plants trees, and they agree that the land and trees become shared property. The commentator explains that this resembles the miller's qafiz (a known analogy in Hanafi fiqh where hiring is paid from the produce). The planting becomes subordinate to the land because both parties consented, so it cannot be removed. The ruling is that the trees belong to the land, and the planter is only entitled to the value of his work and the trees' value if the contract is invalid. However, to make the contract valid (jā'iz), a legal stratagem (ḥīlah) is proposed: the planter sells half the trees to the landowner in exchange for half the land, and then the landowner hires the planter for a nominal wage to tend the entire garden. This stratagem avoids the original prohibition of hiring with uncertain payment. The footnote further clarifies that if the contract is not valid, the trees cannot be surrendered as trees but only as wood, since ownership of trees is tied to the land. A fatwa from Qadi Khan supports the permissibility if the agreement is structured properly.

Key Terms
مساقاة (musāqāh): A contract where one party provides land and the other tends to trees (or plants) in exchange for a share of the produce.
غرس (ghars): Planting or trees that are planted by the worker on the land.
حيلة (ḥīlah): A legal stratagem that transforms a prohibited or invalid transaction into a valid one without violating the law's objectives.

Key Terms from Source Passage

The Hanafi school uses a legal stratagem (sale of trees for land and separate hire) to convert a fasid musaqah contract into a valid one, justified by the qiyas of the miller's qafiz and supported by later fatwas.

Definition and Ruling on Udhiyah (Sacrificial Animal)
Opens Kitab al-Udhiyah with definition, ruling on shares, and linguistic notes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of udhiyah, specify the types of animals permissible for sacrifice, and determine the maximum number of partners in a cow or camel sacrifice.

Core Idea
A sheep serves one person; a cow or camel can be shared by up to seven, each receiving at least one-seventh share.

This passage opens the chapter on Udhiyah, the sacrificial animal of Eid al-Adha. The matn gives the fundamental ruling on shares: one sheep per person, and up to seven persons can share a cow or camel. The commentary then explains the reasoning behind placing this chapter after the book of slaughtering and provides linguistic and juristic definitions. Understanding this ruling is essential for performing the sunnah correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the types of animals and their share limits
The matn states: 'It is a sheep for one person, and a cow or camel from him to seven.' Note that a sheep is for one individual, while a cow or camel can be shared by up to seven people.
This is the basic share ruling.
2
Understand the condition on joint sacrifice
The condition is 'as long as no individual has less than a seventh.' This means each partner's share must be at least one-seventh of the animal.
This condition ensures fairness and prevents minor shares.
3
Explore the linguistic definition and plural forms
The commentator explains that udhiyah linguistically means a sheep slaughtered on the day of sacrifice, and gives plural forms: aḍāḥī, ḍaḥāyā, aḍḥā. This clarifies that the term is used for the animal itself.
Linguistic understanding helps avoid confusion.
4
Determine the juristic definition
According to jurists (as per al-Nihayah), udhiyah is a name for a specific animal: a sheep or goat, or a cow or camel, with the jadhʿ (young sheep) being acceptable, slaughtered with intention of devotion on a specific day (Eid al-Adha and days of tashriq).
This matches the four types: sheep, goat, cow, camel.
5
Note the comparison of virtues between animal types
The commentator compares virtue: a sheep is better than a seventh of a cow if equal in value and meat; a ram is better than a ewe; a female goat is better than a he-goat.
This helps in choosing the best sacrifice when possible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الأضحية
هي شاة من فرد، وبقرة أو بعير منه إلى سبعة إن لم يكن لفرد أقل من سبع (هي شاة من فردِ )، وبقرة أو بعير منه إلى سبعة إن لم يكن لفرد أقل من سبع)
[١]قوله : كتاب الأضحية ؛ أوردَ عقب الذبائح ؛ لأنها ذبيحة خاصة، والذبائح عام، والخاص بعد العام، وتعقب بأنهم إن أرادوا أن الخاص بعد العام في الوجود، فهو ممنوع ؛ لأنه تقرر أن لا وجود للعام إلا في ضمن الخاص، وإن أرادوا في التعقل، فهو إنما يكون إذا كان العام ذاتيّاً للخاص، وكان الخاص معقولاً، كما عرف في موضعه وكون الأمر كذلك ممنوع فيما نحن فيه.
ويمكن أن يجاب تميز الذاتي من العرضي إنّما يتعسَّرُ في الحقائق النفسانية، وأما في الأمور الوضعية والاعتبارية كما نحن فيه ، فكلُّ من اعتبر داخلاً في مفهوم شيء يكون ذاتياً له، ويكون تصور ذلك الشيء تصوّراً له بالكلية، ولا شك أن معنى الأضحية داخل في معنى الأضحية، فتوقف تعلّقها على تعقل معنى الذبح، فيتم التعريف على
اختيار الشق الثاني. وهي في اللغة كما في «النهاية»: شاة نحرها تذبح في يوم الأضحية، ولا يخالفه ما في «القاموس» و«الصحاح» من أنها شاة من غير لفظ : نحرها ؛ لأن لفظ النحر مراد بدليل الأضحية.
وتجمع على أضاحي بالتشديد، ويقال : أضحية وضحايا، كهدية وهدايا ،
ويقال : أضحاة وتجمع على أضحى. وعند الفقهاء كما في «النهاية»: اسم لحيوان مخصوص، وهي الشاة فصاعداً من هذه الأنواع الأربعة والجذع من الضأن تذبح بنية القربة في يوم مخصوص، أشار إليه المصنف له بقوله : وهي شاة... الخ. [٢] قوله : هي . شاة من من فرد ؛ الشاة أفضل من سبع البقرة إذا استويا في القيمة واللحم، وإن كان سبع البقرة أكثر قيمة كان أفضل والكبش أفضل من النعجة، وإن كانت النعجة أكثر قيمة أو لحماً فهي أفضل والأنثى من المعز أفضل من التيس، إذا
Book of Udhiyah. It is a sheep from a single individual, and a cow or camel from him to seven as long as no individual has less than a seventh. [Commentary:] His saying: Book of Udhiyah; it was placed after the book of slaughtering because it is a specific slaughter, and slaughtering is general, and the specific comes after the general. It was objected that if they mean the specific after the general in existence, it is prevented because it is established that the general does not exist except within the specific. And if they mean in conceptualization, then that is only if the general is essential to the specific and the specific is conceptualized, as known in its place. And that being the case is denied in what we are discussing. And it can be answered: the distinction between essential and accidental is difficult in psycho-meta-physical realities, but in positive and conventional matters like this, whatever is considered included in the concept of a thing is essential to it, and the conception of that thing is a complete conception of it. And there is no doubt that the meaning of udhiyah is included in the meaning of slaughter, so its connection depends on conceptualizing the meaning of slaughter. Thus the definition is complete on the second option. In language, as in al-Nihayah: a sheep slaughtered on the day of Udhiyah. And what is in al-Qamus and al-Sihah that it is a sheep without the word slaughter does not contradict because the word slaughter is intended by the evidence of udhiyah. It is pluralized as adāḥī with tashdīd, and it is said uḍḥiyah and ḍaḥāyā like hadiyah and hadāyā, and it is said aḍḥāh and pluralized as aḍḥá. According to jurists as in al-Nihayah: a name for a specific animal, which is a sheep and above from these four types and the jadhʿ of sheep, slaughtered with the intention of devotion on a specific day. The author indicated it by his saying: 'It is a sheep...' etc. His saying: 'It is a sheep from a single individual': the sheep is better than a seventh of a cow if they are equal in value and meat, and if a seventh of a cow is more valuable it is better. A ram is better than a ewe, and if the ewe is more valuable or has more meat then it is better. A female goat is better than a he-goat, if...
Source pages p. 246

Commentary
This passage opens the Book of Udhiyah (Sacrificial Animal) with a definition and ruling. The matn states: 'It is a sheep for one person, and a cow or camel for up to seven, provided no individual has less than a seventh.' The commentator then discusses why this chapter follows the chapter on slaughtering (al-Dhaba'ih), addressing an objection about the ordering. He explains that in conventional matters, the specific (udhiyah) is essentially part of the general (slaughtering), so it is appropriate to place the specific after the general. Linguistically, udhiyah refers to a sheep slaughtered on the day of sacrifice, and the term is used for any sacrificial animal. The commentator provides plural forms and notes that according to jurists, it is any of the four specified types: sheep, goat, cow, or camel, with the jadhʿ (young sheep) being acceptable. The ruling on shares: a sheep suffices for one person; a cow or camel can be shared by up to seven, provided each share is at least one-seventh. The commentator then compares the virtue of different animals: a sheep is better than a seventh of a cow if equal in value and meat; a ram is better than a ewe; a female goat is better than a he-goat. The text is cut off before completing the comparison, but the ruling is clear.

Key Terms
أضحية (uḍḥiyah): A sacrificial animal slaughtered on the days of Eid al-Adha for the sake of Allah.
شاة (shāh): A sheep (includes both ram and ewe; also used for goat in some contexts, but here specifically a sheep).
بعير (baʿīr): A camel (male or female).
جذع (jadhaʿ): A young sheep that has reached the age of one year (or six months if strong, according to some).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How many people can share in sacrificing a cow according to the Hanafi ruling in this passage?
A) Up to 3 people
B) Up to 5 people
C) Up to 7 people, provided each share is at least one-seventh
D) Up to 10 people

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits up to seven persons to share a cow or camel for udhiyah, provided each receives at least one-seventh share; a sheep is for one person only.

Dividing Meat of Udhiyah: Weighing and the Mixed Parts Exception
Ruling on dividing meat with exception for mixed parts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the default ruling for dividing udhiyah meat and identify the exception when mixed with trotters or skin.

Core Idea
Uḍḥiyah meat must be divided by weight; only when mixed with different parts (trotters/skin) is estimation allowed.

This passage from the sharh clarifies a key practical point in the distribution of sacrificial meat among partners. While the general rule requires exact weighing to ensure fairness, the jurists recognize an exception when the meat is accompanied by parts like trotters or skin, as it then becomes a transaction between different categories.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
The meat of udhiyah must be divided by weight (وزناً), not by estimation (جزافاً). This is the default requirement.
2
Note the exception
When the meat is mixed with other parts such as trotters (أكارع) or skin (جلد), the division can be by estimation.
The exception is not for any mixture; only with different categories (meat vs. other parts).
3
Understand the rationale
The exception applies because it is considered a transaction between different genera (صرف الجنس إلى خلاف الجنس), making estimation permissible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الأضحية تعريفها وشروطها وأحكامها ويُقسم اللحْمُ وزناً لا جزافاً إلا إذا ضُمّ معه من أكارعه أو جلده) : أي يكون مع اللحم أكارع أو جلد، ففي كل جانب شيء من اللحم وشيءٌ من الأكارع ، أو يكون في كلِّ جانب شيءٌ من اللحم، وبعض من الجلد، أو يكون في جانب لَحْمٌ وأركاع، وفي آخر لحم وجلد، وإنما يجوزُ صَرْفاً للجنس إلى خلافِ الجنس.
The Book of Udhiyah: its definition, conditions, and rulings. And the meat is divided by weight, not by estimation, unless trotters or its skin are included with it. That is: trotters or skin are with the meat, so in each side there is some meat and some trotters, or in each side some meat and some skin, or in one side meat and trotters and in another meat and skin. And it is permissible as a deviation of the genus to a different genus.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage from the sharh explains a ruling from the matn: the meat of the udhiyah must be divided by weight (وزنا), not by estimation (جزاف), to ensure exact fairness among partners. However, an exception is allowed when the meat is mixed with parts of a different category, such as trotters (أكارع) or skin (جلد). The commentator elaborates that the exception applies when each portion contains a mixture of meat and these other parts, so it is considered a transaction between different types of goods, which is permissible without precise weighing.

Key Terms
جزاف (jizaf): estimation or guesswork without exact measurement, used for dividing butcher meat
أكارع (akari'): the trotters or lower legs of an animal

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, how must the meat of udhiyah be divided?
A) By estimation according to custom
B) By weight unless trotters or skin are included
C) By volume
D) Equally without weighing

Key Takeaway
Uḍḥiyah meat must be divided by weight; estimation is only allowed when mixed with different parts like trotters or skin, as it becomes a transaction between different categories.

Subscription in a Cow for Udhiyah: Istihsan Overrides Qiyas
Ruling on joint subscription in a cow for udhiyah with proof from istihsan

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school permits joint subscription (ishtirak) in a cow for udhiyah before purchase, and apply the principle of istihsan to distinguish between the ruling by qiyas and the preferred ruling by istihsan.

Core Idea
Necessity overrides strict analogy: a cow intended for udhiyah may still be shared before slaughter if partners are unavailable at purchase.

This lesson examines a case where the Hanafi school applies istihsan (juristic preference) to permit a transaction that pure qiyas would forbid. The issue is joint subscription in a single cow for udhiyah. While the cow is dedicated to worship and normally cannot be resold, practical necessity allows pre-purchase sharing.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary ruling
State the Hanafi ruling: subscription in a fat cow for udhiyah is valid (sahih) by istihsan.
The text opens with 'Wa sahha ishtirak...' — it is valid.
2
Understand the qiyas objection
By strict analogy (qiyas), it is impermissible because the cow is prepared for worship (qurbah) and cannot be sold. This is the view of Zufar.
The analogy: a dedicated sacrificial animal cannot be sold, so shares cannot be sold after intention.
3
Analyze the istihsan justification
The istihsan is based on necessity: one may find a fat cow but lack partners at the moment of purchase. Delaying may lose the opportunity, so sharing is permitted.
The text: 'قد يجد بقرة سمينة، ولا يجد الشركاء وقت البيع، فالحاجة ماسة إلى هذا'.
4
Note the preferred timing
Subscription before purchase is more beloved (ahab), after purchase is disliked (yukrah) according to Abu Hanifa.
This shows a gradation in desirability, not in validity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وصح اشتراك سنّة في بقرة مشرية لأضحية استحساناً)، وفي القياس لا يجوز، وهو قولُ زُفَر الله ؛ لأنه أعدها للقُرْبة ، فلا يجوز بيعها، وجه الاستحسان : قد يجد بقرة سمينة، ولا يجد الشركاء وقت البيع، فالحاجة ماسة إلى هذا، (وذا قبل الشراء أحب) ، ذا إشارة إلى الاشتراك ، وعن أبي حنيفة له يُكْرَهُ الاشتراك بعد الشراء.
And a subscription for one year in a fat cow for udhiyah is valid by istihsan. By qiyas it is not permissible, and this is the opinion of Zufar (may Allah have mercy on him), because it was prepared for worship, so it is not permissible to sell it. The reason for istihsan: sometimes one finds a fat cow and cannot find partners at the time of sale, so there is pressing need for this. And 'that before purchase is more beloved' – 'that' refers to the subscription, and from Abu Hanifa it is disliked to subscribe after purchase.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling on joint subscription (اشتراك) in a single cow for udhiyah. According to the relied-upon Hanafi position based on istihsan (juristic preference), it is valid to subscribe before the purchase, even though qiyas (analogy) would forbid it because the animal is dedicated to worship and thus cannot be sold. Zufar's opinion follows the qiyas. The istihsan is justified by practical need: a person may find a desirable fat cow but lack partners at that moment, and delaying may lose the opportunity. The text further notes that such subscription is preferred (أحب) before purchase and disliked (يكره) after purchase, according to Abu Hanifa.

Key Terms
اشتراك (ishtirak): joint subscription where multiple people contribute to purchase a single sacrificial animal
استحسان (istihsan): juristic preference that sets aside a strict analogy due to necessity or custom

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why did the Hanafis permit subscription in a cow for udhiyah before purchase despite the qiyas prohibition?
A) Because the animal is not yet dedicated to worship
B) Due to necessity when a fat cow is found but partners are not present
C) Because Zufar's opinion was considered weak
D) Because the cow can be sold at any time

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, istihsan can override a clear qiyas when necessity demands it, as in the case of pre-purchase subscription for udhiyah.

Preferring the Female Animal for Udhiyah When Value Equal
Preference of female over male animals for udhiyah when value equal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition under which a female camel or cow is preferred over a male for udhiyah and apply it correctly.

Core Idea
Female camels and cows are superior for udhiyah only when equal in price.

When selecting an animal for udhiyah, the gender can influence preference. This passage from Munyat al-Mufti clarifies that if a female and male camel or cow are sold at the same price, the female is considered better. This is a matter of recommended choice (afdaliyyah), not obligation. The ruling helps the believer optimise the quality of their sacrifice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is choosing an animal for udhiyah. The ruling is that the female is better (afdal) when two conditions are met.
2
State the condition
The condition is equality in value (استواء في القيمة). Without this, the preference does not apply.
Note: value here means market price, not other qualities like age or health.
3
Apply the ruling
If you have two animals of the same species, one male and one female, at the same price, choose the female. If the male is cheaper, it may be better to choose it due to financial consideration, but the text does not address that explicitly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
استويا قيمة والأنثى من الإبل والبقر أفضلُ من الذكور إذا استويا في القيمة. كذا في منية المفتي.
If equal in value, the female of camels and cows is better than the male when they are equal in value. Thus in Munyat al-Mufti.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This is a short ruling from Munyat al-Mufti: when choosing between a female and a male camel or cow for udhiyah, and they are equal in market price, the female is considered superior. This is a matter of preference (أفضلية), not obligation. The fat content and taste of female animals are often considered better.

Key Terms
أنثى (untha): female animal (camel or cow)
ذكور (dhukur): male animal

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Always
B) When they are equal in value
C) Only if the female is cheaper
D) Only if the male is unavailable

Key Takeaway
A female camel or cow is preferred for udhiyah only when its market price equals that of the male.

The Obligation of Udhiyah: Hanafi vs. Shafi'i
Explains the obligation of udhiyah with proof and inter-madhhab dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school considers udhiyah wajib and evaluate the Shafi'i counterargument, identifying the practical consequence of the dispute.

Core Idea
A threat in a hadith makes udhiyah wajib for the capable; a sunnah according to Shafi'i.

This lesson explores one of the classic inter-madhhab disputes: whether the Eid sacrifice (udhiyah) is obligatory or merely recommended. The Hanafi school, relying on a hadith with a stern threat, holds it is wajib. The Shafi'i school interprets the same evidence differently, considering it a sunnah mu'akkadah. Understanding the evidence and reasoning of each side deepens your appreciation of how hadiths are weighted and how ikhtilaf arises.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi position
State that the Hanafi school considers udhiyah wajib (obligatory) upon a person who has the capacity, i.e., wealth beyond basic needs. Note that it is not fard, because the evidence is zanni al-thubut.
The relied-upon view is that it is wajib for oneself, not for one's child (zahir al-riwayah).
2
Examine the Hanafi proof
The primary proof is the hadith: 'Whoever has capacity and does not sacrifice, let him not approach our prayer place.' This is an ahad hadith, so its transmission (thubut) is zanni.
The hadith is not mutawatir; thus the ruling is wajib, not fard.
3
Understand the wajh al-dalalah
In Hanafi usul, a threat (wa'id) for omitting an act indicates that the act is obligatory. The threat here—'do not approach the musalla'—is severe, implying the omission is a sin.
This is the key argument: the threat transforms a recommended practice into an obligation.
4
Present the Shafi'i position
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that udhiyah is a sunnah mu'akkadah (one of his two opinions) or even tatawwu' (voluntary). His evidence includes hadiths where the Prophet performed it without commanding it as obligatory, and he interprets the threat as a severe discouragement, not a legal obligation.
The Shafi'i school gives more weight to the absence of an explicit command.
5
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: According to Hanafi, a capable person who deliberately omits udhiyah commits a sin and must repent; according to Shafi'i, he is not sinful but misses great reward. This difference impacts fatwa in daily practice.
The Hanafi view is mufta bihi in the school; the opposite is also respected.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا تجب إلا على من عليه الفطرة لنفسه لا لطفله في ظاهر الرواية. والمعتبر في غناء الفطرة والأضحية من كتب الفقه: ما زاد على الواحد، ومن التفاسير والأحاديث ما زاد على الاثنين، ومن المصاحف: لمن يحسن القراءة، ما زاد على الواحد. وقيل: كلّ ذلك معتبر في كتب الطب والأدب كلّها معتبرة في الغنى، فيتعلّق بهذا النصاب أحكام وجوب صدقة الفطر والأضحية، وحرمة وضع الزكاة فيه، ووجوب نفقة الأقارب، ومن أراد زيادة تفصيل فلينظر في أول باب الفطرة والأضحية من «فتاوى قاضي خان». وإنما تجب لقوله عليه السلام: «من وجد سعة ولم يضح، فلا يقربن مصلانا». وعند الشافعي هي سنة مؤكدة وهو أحد قوليه، وفي قوله الآخر: تطوع، وروى ابن زياد عن أبي حنيفة، وابن رستم عن محمد: إنها فريضة. وفي رواية الحسن عن أبي حنيفة رحمه الله تجب لطفله كما في الفطرة. قلنا: سبب الفطرة رأس يمونه ويلي عليه، وهما موجودان في الصغير. وقوله: سبب الفطرة رأس يمونه؛ من مانَ يمون موناً: إذا حمل مؤنته، وقام بكفايته. وقوله: ويلي عليه؛ من الولاية، وهما موجودان في الصغير.
And it is not obligatory except on the one upon whom is the fitra for himself, not for his child, in the apparent narration. And what is considered for wealth sufficiency for fitra and udhiyah from the books of fiqh: what exceeds one (person's need), and from tafsir and hadith books: what exceeds two, and from mushafs: for one who reads well, what exceeds one. And some said: all that is considered; in medical and literary books all are considered for wealth. Connected to this minimal threshold are rulings of obligation of fitra and udhiyah, prohibition of placing zakat, and obligation of spending on relatives. Whoever wants further detail should look at the beginning of the chapter of fitra and udhiyah from Fatawa Qadikhan. And it is obligatory due to his saying, peace be upon him: 'Whoever has capacity and does not sacrifice, let him not approach our prayer place.' And according to al-Shafi'i it is a sunnah mu'akkadah, which is one of his two statements, and in his other statement: a voluntary act. And Ibn Ziyad narrated from Abu Hanifa, and Ibn Rustam from Muhammad: it is a fard. And in the narration of al-Hasan from Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, it is obligatory for his child as in fitra. We said: the reason for fitra is a dependent head whom he supports, and guardianship, both of which exist for the child. And his statement: the reason for fitra is a dependent head; from mana yamunu: to bear his maintenance and undertake his support. And his statement: 'and guardianship' from wilaya, and both exist for the child.
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage discusses the obligation (وجوب) of udhiyah. According to the zahir al-riwayah, it is wajib only on a person who is obligated to pay fitra for himself, not for his child. The text then defines wealth sufficiency (غنى) for these obligations, noting different criteria from various disciplines: in fiqh, wealth beyond one's basic needs; in tafsir and hadith, beyond two; and for a reciter of Quran, beyond one mushaf. These differences indicate the nuance in defining capacity. The primary proof for the obligation is the hadith: 'Whoever has means and does not sacrifice, let him not approach our prayer place.' This is a clear threat, indicating wujub in the Hanafi school. The passage then presents the inter-madhhab dispute: Imam al-Shafi'i considers udhiyah a sunnah mu'akkadah (one of his two positions) or a tatawwu' (voluntary). Within the Hanafi school, there is also an intra-madhhab ikhtilaf: the zahir al-riwayah restricts obligation to oneself, but a narration from al-Hasan from Abu Hanifa makes it wajib for one's child as well, akin to fitra. The response (قلنا) clarifies that fitra is linked to supporting a dependent (رأس يمونه) and having guardianship (ولاية), both of which exist for a child, so the analogy with fitra holds. However, the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is the one from zahir al-riwayah.

Key Terms
فطرة (fitra): obligatory charity (sadaqat al-fitr) at the end of Ramadan
أضحية (udhiya): the animal sacrificed on Eid al-Adha
غنى (ghina): wealth sufficiency that triggers obligation of fitra and udhiyah
رأس يمونه (ra's yamunuhu): a dependent whose maintenance the person provides

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the zahir al-riwayah, who must perform udhiyah?
A) Any Muslim who can afford it, including for his dependents
B) Only the person who is obligated to pay fitra for himself, not for his child
C) Every family head
D) Only wealthy individuals who own a nisab

Key Takeaway
Udhiyah is wajib in Hanafi law based on a threat in a hadith, but the relied-upon position limits it to the individual, not dependents.

Ruling on Udhiyah for a Minor: Not Obligatory in the Apparent Narration
Passage states ruling, proof, and disputes on udhiyah for a child.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on udhiyah for a minor child according to the Hanafi school and explain the basis for the intra-madhhab difference.

Core Idea
Udhiyah is not wajib for a child in Zahir al-Riwayah because it is a pure worship, unlike fitrah.

This passage addresses whether a guardian must sacrifice an animal on behalf of his minor child. While fitrah is obligatory for the guardian on behalf of dependents, the Hanafi school distinguishes udhiyah as a pure devotional act. The apparent narration (Zahir al-Riwayah) holds it is not obligatory for the child, but a minority narration from al-Hasan ibn Ziyad says it is. We will walk through the proof, the rebuttal, and the practical procedure for performing udhiyah on a child's behalf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base ruling
Locate the sentence that states the ruling for the child in Zahir al-Riwayah: 'ولا تجب إلا على من عليه الفطرة لنفسه لا لطفله' – udhiyah is not obligatory for the child.
This is the relied-upon position.
2
Understand the proof for adult obligation
The dalil for udhiyah's wujub on adults is the hadith: 'مَن وَجَدَ سَعَةً وَلَمْ يُضَحِّ فَلَا يَقْرَبَنَّ مُصَلَّانَا.' The threat indicates obligation for those with capacity.
This hadith is not quoted in the passage but is referenced in the analytical toolbox.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Note the narration from al-Hasan ibn Ziyad from Abu Hanifa that udhiyah is obligatory for the child like fitrah. Contrast with the majority view (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) in Zahir al-Riwayah.
The commentator rebuts by distinguishing the sabab of fitrah.
4
Distinguish udhiyah from fitrah
Examine the reasoning: fitrah is owed because of the guardian's duty to support (ra's yamoonahu wa yali alayh). Udhiyah is a pure worship (qurbah mahdah) that cannot be obligated on one person due to another.
This distinction is key to understanding why the child is not obligated.
5
Determine the operative position and procedure
The mufta bihi is the Zahir al-Riwayah: no obligation on the child. If the guardian wishes to sacrifice, Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf say from the child's property (if the child has wealth); Muhammad says from the guardian's own. Leftover meat after the child eats is exchanged for durable items.
This covers both the ruling and its practical application.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: ولا تجب إلا على من عليه الفطرة لنفسه لا لطفله في ظاهر الرواية. وعند الشافعي هي سنة. وفي رواية الحسن عن أبي حنيفة تجب لطفله كما في الفطرة. قلنا: سبب الفطرة رأس يمونه ويلي عليه، وهما موجودان في الصغير بخلاف الأضحية، فإنها عبادة وقربة محضة، والأصل فيها أن لا يجب على الغير بسبب الغير؛ ولهذا لا يجب عن عبده، وإن كان تجب عنه صدقة الفطر. بل يضحي عنه أبوه أو وصيه من ماله. هذا عند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف. وقال محمد والشافعي: يضح عنه أبوه من مال نفسه لا من ماله. وأكل منه الطفل، وما بقي يُبَدِّلُ بما ينتفع بعينه: كالثوب، والخف لا بما ينتفع به بالاستهلاك كالخبز، ونحوه.
His statement: 'And it is not obligatory except upon the one upon whom fitrah is obligatory for himself, not for his child in the apparent narration.' According to al-Shafi'i it is sunnah. In the narration of al-Hasan from Abu Hanifa, it is obligatory for his child just like fitrah. We said: The cause of fitrah is a head whom he supports and over whom he has guardianship, and both are present in the minor, unlike udhiyah, for it is an act of worship and pure devotion, and the principle regarding devotions is that they are not obligatory upon another due to another; for this reason it is not obligatory for his slave, even though fitrah is obligatory for him. Rather, his father or executor sacrifices for him from his property. This is according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. Muhammad and al-Shafi'i said: His father sacrifices for him from his own property, not from the child's property. And the child eats from it, and whatever remains is exchanged for something whose benefit is in its substance: like a garment and a shoe, not for something whose benefit is through consumption like bread and the like.
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling of udhiyah (sacrificial animal) for a minor child. It begins by stating the ruling from the apparent narration (Zahir al-Riwayah) that udhiyah is not obligatory for a child, just as fitrah is not obligatory on behalf of a child in the apparent narration (though fitrah is obligatory on the guardian for the child). The proof for udhiyah's obligation on an adult is a hadith: 'Whoever has capacity and does not sacrifice, let him not approach our prayer place.' Imam al-Shafi'i considers udhiyah a sunnah. A narration from al-Hasan ibn Ziyad from Abu Hanifa says it is obligatory for the child just like fitrah. The commentator rebuts this by distinguishing the cause of fitrah: the guardian supports the child (ra's yamoonahu wa yali alayh), which is present in the child, but udhiyah is a pure act of worship (qurbah mahdah) that cannot be obligated due to another person. It is not obligated for a slave even though fitrah is. Then the passage addresses who performs the sacrifice: according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, the father or executor sacrifices from the child's property if the child has wealth; Muhammad and al-Shafi'i say from the father's own property. Finally, if the child eats from the sacrifice, the remaining meat is sold and the proceeds used to buy something durable like a garment or shoes, not consumables like bread, because the meat was for consumption and exchanging for consumables would be wasteful.

Key Terms
الأضحية (al-udhiyah): The sacrificial animal offered on the days of Eid al-Adha as an act of worship.
رأس يمونه ويلي عليه (ra's yamoonahu wa yali alayh): A person whom one supports financially and over whom one has legal guardianship.
قربة محضة (qurbah mahdah): A pure act of worship with no worldly benefit, like udhiyah as opposed to fitrah which has a benefit for the poor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the apparent narration (Zahir al-Riwayah), is udhiyah obligatory for a minor child?
A) Yes, it is obligatory just like fitrah.
B) No, it is not obligatory.
C) It is sunnah.
D) It depends on whether the child has wealth.

Key Takeaway
Udhiyah is not obligatory for a minor child in the Hanafi school's relied-upon narration because it is a pure act of worship, distinguished from fitrah.

Sacrificing from a Minor's Wealth: The Hanafi Dispute
Explains the disagreement over sacrificing from a minor's wealth

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on sacrificing udhiyah from a minor's wealth, explain the analogy with sadaqat al-fitr, and evaluate the opposing argument.

Core Idea
Udhiyah from a minor's wealth hinges on whether it resembles fitr or is a pure devotion.

Should a father sacrifice for his minor child from the child's own money or from his own pocket? This question sparked a dispute between the great Hanafi imams and their Shafi'i counterpart. The debate turns on the nature of udhiyah: is it a financial devotion like sadaqat al-fitr, or a pure act of worship that cannot be imposed on one person for another? In this lesson, you'll explore the reasoning behind each position and the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf).

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
Read the first sentence: 'بل يضحي عنه أبوه أو وصيه من ماله، هذا عند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف له، وقال محمد والشافعي: يضح عنه أبوه من مال نفسه لا من ماله.' Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf permit using the minor's wealth; Muhammad and al-Shafi'i require the father to pay from his own.
The phrase 'والفتوى على ما في المتن' indicates the relied-upon position later.
2
Understand the opposing proof
Muhammad and al-Shafi'i argue that udhiyah is a pure act of worship (qurbah mahdah) and the principle is: acts of worship are not obligatory on one person due to another. Hence the father cannot use the minor's wealth; he must sacrifice from his own.
This principle is invoked also for a slave: udhiyah is not obligatory from a slave's wealth, unlike fitr.
3
Follow the Hanafi analogy
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf draw analogy with sadaqat al-fitr and maintenance (nafaqa). The sabab (cause) for fitr is the guardian's authority and maintenance obligation, which exist for the minor. The same cause applies to udhiyah because both involve financial devotion. The bloodshed element does not negate the financial aspect.
The text says: 'قلنا: سبب الفطرة رأس يمونه ويلي عليه؛ وهما موجودان في الصغير'.
4
Note the key principle
The principle 'الأصل في القرب أن لا يجب على الغير بسبب الغير' is used by both sides. The Hanafis counter that this principle is overridden here by the specific evidence of guardianship, just as in nafaqa.
The distinction is that fitr and udhiyah have a financial component that ties them to maintenance.
5
Determine the practical difference
If the minor has wealth, Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf allow using that wealth for the sacrifice; Muhammad and al-Shafi'i insist the father pays himself. This affects families where the minor has inherited or gifted wealth.
This is the thamarah al-khilaf: the actual cost is borne differently.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الأضحية تعريفها وشروطها وأحكامها
بل يضحي عنه أبوه أو وصيه من ماله
(۱) رأس يمونه ويلي عليه (۲) بل يضحي عنه أبوه (۳) أو وصيه من ماله، هذا عند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف له، وقال محمد والشَّافِعِي : يضح عنه أبوه من مال نفسه لا من ماله.
بخلاف الأضحية، فإنّها عبادة وقربة محضة، والأصل فيها أن لا يجب على الغيرِ بسبب الغير؛ ولهذا لا يجب عن عبده، وإن كان تجب عنه صدقة الفطر. «جلبي [١]قوله : قلنا : سبب الفطرة رأس يمونه ويلي عليه ؛ وهما موجودان في الصغير، والأضحية قربة محضة؛ لأنّ الإراقة قربة من العبد للربّ من غير شائبة ومشاركة، ولا كذلك التصدق بالمال؛ لأنَّ المالَ كما يتقرب به إلى الله تعالى يتقرب به إلى العبد، والأصلُ في القرب أن لا يجب على الغير بسبب الغير؛ ولهذا لا يجب الأضحية من عبده، وإن كان عنه صدقة الفطر. «علوي».
[٢] قوله : بل يضحي عنه أبوه ...الخ ؛ قال الشارح : هذا عند أبي حنيفة لله وأبي يوسف الله، وقال محمد والشافعي: يضحي عنه أبوه من مال نفسه لا من ماله، فالخلافُ في هذا كالخلاف في صدقة الفطر.
وقيل في «باب صدقة الفطر»: إن كان للصغار مالٌ يؤدى من مالهم عند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف خلافاً لمحمد الله؛ لأنَّ الشرع أجراه مجرى المؤنة، فأشبه النفقة. وقيل: لا يجوزُ الأضحية من مال الصغير في قولهم؛ لأن القربة تتأدى بالإراقة
(۱) من الولاية، وهما موجودان في الصغير بخلاف الأضحية، فإنها عبادة وقربة محضة، والأصل فيها أن لا يجب على الغير بسبب الغير؛ ولهذا لا يجب عن عبده، وإن كان تجب عنه صدقة الفطر. ينظر : «ذخيرة العقبى (ص٥٧٣).
The book of al-Udhiyah: its definition, conditions, and rulings. Rather, his father or executor sacrifices from his wealth. (1) head of his dependents and he has authority over him (2) rather, his father sacrifices for him (3) or his executor from his wealth; this is according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, may Allah be pleased with them. And Muhammad and al-Shafi'i said: His father sacrifices for him from his own wealth, not from his (the minor's) wealth. Unlike al-Udhiyah, for it is pure worship and devotion, and the principle is that it is not obligatory upon another due to another; hence it is not obligatory from his slave, although sadaqat al-fitr is obligatory from him. Jalabi: He said: we say: the cause of fitr is the head of dependents and authority over them; both exist in the minor, while al-Udhiyah is pure devotion because the shedding of blood is a devotion from the slave to the Lord without any admixture or partnership, and giving wealth is not like that because wealth is used to draw near to Allah and also to the slave. The principle regarding devotions is that they are not obligatory upon another due to another; hence al-Udhiyah is not obligatory from his slave, even though sadaqat al-fitr is from him. 'Alawi. [2] He said: Rather, his father sacrifices for him, etc. The commentator said: This is according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, may Allah be pleased with them, and Muhammad and al-Shafi'i said: His father sacrifices for him from his own wealth, not from his. The disagreement here is like the disagreement in sadaqat al-fitr. And it was said in the chapter of sadaqat al-fitr: If minors have wealth, it is given from their wealth according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, contrary to Muhammad, may Allah be pleased with him, because the Law considers it as a maintenance expense, so it resembles nafaqa. And it was said: al-Udhiyah from the minor's wealth is not allowed in their view, because the devotion is fulfilled by the shedding of blood (1) from authority, and both exist in the minor, unlike al-Udhiyah, for it is worship and pure devotion, and the principle is that it is not obligatory upon another due to another; hence it is not obligatory from his slave, even though sadaqat al-fitr is obligatory from him. See: Dhakhirat al-Uqba (p. 573).
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage is a inter-madhhab dispute regarding sacrificing a udhiyah on behalf of a minor child. The source text presents two positions: Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that the sacrifice should be made from the minor's own wealth, if he has any, because the father or executor acts as a guardian. Muhammad al-Shaybani and al-Shafi'i hold that the father must sacrifice from his own wealth, not from the minor's. The commentator explains that the disagreement parallels the one in sadaqat al-fitr, where Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf allow paying from the minor's wealth while Muhammad requires the guardian to pay from his own. The reasoning is that the cause (sabab) for fitr is the guardian's authority and maintenance obligation, which exist for the minor, but udhiyah is a pure devotion of bloodshed, which is not connected to maintenance. The text also notes that the principle is that acts of worship are not obligated on one person due to another, hence udhiyah is not obligatory from a slave, unlike fitr. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is not explicitly stated here, but the text mentions that the fatwa is according to what is in the matn, which implies the view of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.

Key Terms
الأضحية (al-udhiyah): The sacrificial animal slaughtered on the day of Eid al-Adha as an act of worship.
رأس يمونه (ra's yamunuhu): A person whom one is obligated to support financially, such as a minor child.
ولاية (wilayah): Guardianship or authority over a minor, which includes financial decision-making.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi mufta bihi position, from whose wealth should the udhiyah sacrifice for a minor be made if the minor possesses wealth?
A) From the father's wealth.
B) From the minor's own wealth.
C) From the executor's wealth if there is no father.
D) It is not permissible to sacrifice for a minor.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits sacrificing udhiyah from a minor's wealth by analogy with sadaqat al-fitr, based on guardianship and maintenance, despite the opposing view that it is a pure devotion.
